==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This master’s thesis delves into the prediction of heat stress in low-income housing in
South Africa, with a specific focus on the implications of climate change. The research is
framed within the projected climate scenario from the Intergovernmental Panel for Climate
Change (IPCC) and identifies the potential of machine learning models in predicting heat
stress. This potential is explored through a hybrid dataset composed of on-site measure-
ments and simulation data.
The study employs a blend of building energy simulation (BES) and machine learning
models, leveraging artificial intelligence to address the scarcity of on-site measurement
data. The methodology involves simulating a range of building characteristics and oc-
cupant behaviours to compute the thermal environment in a simplified building energy
model. The results of these simulations are then used in a k-means clustering approach
to augment the on-site measurement dataset. This strategy aims to utilize the available
measured data in training a classification model for heat stress prediction. However, a
discernible gap is identified when comparing the Principal Component Analysis projection
between the measured and simulated data, likely due to the extreme simplification of the
simulated model. This finding underscores the need for increased complexity in simula-
tions and improved quality of measured data. Despite this, the method shows promise in
using a clustering approach to simplify building and occupant characteristics into cluster
labels as a proxy.
The research explores the use of various classifiers, with the Bagging Classifier and Ran-
dom Forest Classifier emerging as the most effective, achieving accuracies of 91% and
87% respectively. These results were obtained after fine-tuning in a 5-fold cross-validation
and using down-sampling to address the class imbalance, leading to a more relevant ac-
curacy compared to other related studies.
The thesis concludes by emphasizing the potential of machine learning models, partic-
ularly Ensemble models such as Bagging and Random Forest, in predicting heat stress
exposure in dwellings for use in Early Alerting Systems. The use of simulated data with
weather files that take into account the IPCC’s projections could enable better accuracy in
predictions under future climate scenarios. Furthermore, more advanced models, such as
forecasting machine learning models, have the potential to be explored in future studies
to predict heat stress directly from historical data, instead of relying on weather forecasts
with developed simple machine learning models. However, this approach would likely re-
quire more data on dwellings, which is a challenging prospect in the context of low-income
housing.
In conclusion, while the study identifies significant potential in Ensemble models such as
Random Forest and Bagging for predicting heat stress, further research is needed in the
African context for Early Warning Systems in the face of increasing heat stress events
alongside using passive design solutions such as shading, night cooling and coping with
overcrowding in dwellings to decrease the risks.

1
Introduction
The increasing frequency of extreme heat events from 1990 onward is strongly linked
to climate change [1]. Notably, awareness about the anthropogenic origins of climate
change and its consequences began to rise in the early nineties, highlighted by the Earth
Summit in Rio, Brazil. This development followed tentative political actions initiated in
the seventies at the first Human Environment Conference in Stockholm. Both summits
shed light on key climate change issues and advanced studies on global warming and
the depletion of fossil resources, issues which have been further complicated by recent
geopolitical events leading to fossil oil shortages [2].
Amid these changes, maintaining quality housing conditions is becoming crucial for peo-
ple’s well-being, especially considering the increasing global temperatures and associ-
ated heat stress. As emphasized by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change
(IPCC), heat-related health issues and mortality are growing global concerns. They pre-
dict a significant temperature rise across the African continent, which underscores the
critical situation humanity is facing. Global warming is inevitable, and immediate, strate-
gic action is needed to mitigate its adverse effects.
To address this urgency, the IPCC has laid out a strategic pathway for adaptations to
extreme heat events. While the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions remains a primary
recommendation in their sixth assessment report, policymakers are also being urged to
act swiftly. The European Union (EU) Commission, in harmony with these global efforts,
has introduced a framework under the EU Adaptation Strategy. This strategy encourages
local policymakers to develop and implement local plans and solutions to adapt to climate
change and combat extreme heat events [3].
Nonetheless, the recent IPCC report [4] stresses that African countries face substantial
challenges in combating climate change. The impacts of extreme heat on Africa have
largely been neglected, which calls for collective efforts to boost resilience across the
continent. Neglecting to take action could lead to catastrophic consequences for local
populations and neighboring countries, including increased migration and geopolitical
complications, as warned by North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO) [5].
Extreme heat events can lead to serious health issues ranging from fatigue and learning
difficulties to heat strokes [6] [7]. Continued exposure to such risks may not only trigger
migration but could also intensify poverty, leaving large areas globally underdeveloped.
Solutions are needed to prevent these effects, helping people to develop habits and adapt
their buildings to reduce indoor heat risks. This is especially important given that indoor
temperatures are often higher than outdoor temperatures [8] [9].
Against this backdrop, this thesis seeks to contribute to developing solutions for the press-
ing issue of heat stress in Africa, which is further exacerbated by climate change. The
research aims to leverage artificial intelligence to assess indoor thermal quality in sub-
Saharan buildings subjected to climate change. The specific objectives of this study are:
1. To employ machine learning and/or artificial intelligence in predicting thermal con-
ditions in sub-Saharan buildings. This would involve determining how various build-
ing characteristics in sub-Saharan climates contribute to heat stress in two studied
settings, dense urban and rural settings, respectively, Johannesburg and Giyani in
Limpopo Province.
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
1
==PAGE:16==
2. To utilize existing data sets from studies conducted in South Africa, alongside Build-
ing Energy Simulation (BES), to create an expansive data set for sub-Saharan build-
ings. This data set would consider different building characteristics based on typolo-
gies of buildings prevalent in the sub-Saharan context.
3. To explore state-of-art machine learning models (i.e. Decision Tree) that are trained
on the aforementioned data sets, enabling predictions of thermal stress on occu-
pants based on indoor air temperature and relative humidity in dwellings.
4. To assess the accuracy of these models through appropriate performance metrics,
thereby ensuring their reliability in predicting indoor thermal conditions.
5. To pave the way for the future implementation of the developed model in a commu-
nicative tool such as an app or a web app. This tool will provide feedback to inhab-
itants, empowering them to mitigate high heat stress and related health hazards.
This method is considered an Early Alerting System (EAS) and enables inhabitants
to take action to protect themselves against potential hazards.
6. To evaluate and include future climate predictions to pave the way for using machine
learning models in future scenarios. Assess differences between the present-day
and future conditions and their relevance in terms of heat exposure of occupants
inside dwellings. The building characteristics’ influence on thermal conditions will
be discussed between present-day and future climate conditions to give insights into
protective strategies.
The questions that this thesis will answer are:
1. Can Building Energy Simulation (BES) and on-site measurements be associated
with developing an accurate machine learning model and leveraging sparse data
issues?
2. How to reduce the complexity of building and occupant characteristics to be easily
interpreted by occupants themselves?
3. How accurately can the machine learning models predict indoor heat stress?
4. How does climate change influence indoor heat stress?
By addressing these objectives and questions, this master’s thesis aims to contribute
significantly to the field of heat stress prevention by using artificial intelligence to assess
heat stress and cope with its increasing occurrences due to climate change, particularly
in the context of sub-Saharan Africa. A review of state-of-art research will be the focus of
Section 2.

Literature review
The literature review conducted for this thesis was primarily executed through DTU Findit,
utilizing a strategic selection of keywords which included: Machine Learning, Indoor Cli-
mate, Building Simulation, Prediction, Artificial Intelligence, Climate Change, Human Health-
Risks, Thermal Comfort, Low-Income Housing, Indoor temperature, Heat Stress, South
Africa, Building Characteristics, Heat Stress Adaptations, Heat Waves. These keywords
were used separately or combined, for example (non-exhaustive):
• Artificial Intelligence AND Indoor Climate,
• Machine Learning AND Indoor Climate,
• Machine Learning AND Thermal Comfort,
• Machine Learning AND Indoor Temperature,
• Machine Learning AND Heat Stress,
• Heat Stress AND Human Health-Risks,
• Low-Income Housing AND (Heat Stress OR Thermal Comfort),
• Building Simulation AND (Indoor Temperature OR Heat Stress) AND Prediction.
From this search, a total of 59 scientific articles were identified. Among these, 56 were
found to be pertinent to at least one of the central themes of this thesis: thermal comfort,
machine learning, African context, heat stress, and artificial intelligence. The literature
under review spans from 2000 to 2023, with the highest concentration of publications
observed in 2019, as depicted in Figure 2.1. The primary geographical focus of the studies
was Africa, complemented by select research related to Southern Europe, the Americas,
and South, Southeast Asia in similar climate or socio-economical settings.

2.1
Exposure to Extreme Heat
Exposure to extreme heat has emerged as a significant concern in studies of indoor en-
vironments. Its relevance among scientists amplified notably after the 2003 European
heatwave, which resulted in an estimated 70 000 heat-related deaths in Europe [10].
This extreme event disproportionately affected vulnerable groups such as the elderly.
The issue of extreme heat exposure further garnered attention during the construction of
stadiums for the 2022 Qatar World Cup, where cardiac mortality among migrant workers
was reportedly linked to extreme heat exposure amidst sub-optimal working conditions
[11].
Studies have repeatedly underscored the increased mortality risk associated with high-
temperature exposure [12]. One such research highlights the significant increase in mor-
tality risk even with minor temperature increases. Although the death toll attributed to high
temperatures is lower than that linked to cold temperatures, climate change predictions
suggest a rising trend in heat-related fatalities, such as temperature increases with global
warming. This projection is supported by data from Cameroon’s main airport weather
stations, which indicate an annual temperature increase of 0.0284°C and more frequent
occurrences of indoor temperature peaks around 38°C [6].
4
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:19==
Furthermore, heat-related mortality risk has been identified to spike during heatwave pe-
riods, mainly due to consecutive days of extreme heat rather than isolated temperature
peaks [7]. These findings underscore the need for public intervention to assess and min-
imize heat-related health risks, especially during heat waves.
Despite studies about heatwaves and heat-related fatalities, there is no standardized heat-
wave definition. For example, World Health Organization (WHO) defines a heatwave as
hot weather that can last for several days [10]. This lack of parameters to assess heat-
waves and exposure to extreme heat could potentially result in thousands of deaths as
described in [10].
Notably, exposure to extreme heat events is not uniform across populations.
Urban
dwellers, for instance, are more prone to heat exposure, underscoring the need for risk
mitigation through urban planning and reduced population density [13]. Geographic dis-
parities are also evident, with a study from 2001 to 2008 pinpointing Europe as more
heat-vulnerable compared to the United States [14]. Moreover, countries’ responses to
heat exposure vary. While South Asia has implemented heat action plans, such measures
are not universally adopted across susceptible regions [8] despite the urging need.
This discrepancy is particularly pronounced in Africa, heavily impacted by extreme heat
exposure. In Cameroon, a study highlighted the correlation between heat exposure and
compromised mental performance and school attendance, marked by symptoms such as
fatigue, thirst, and headaches [6].
Finally, the challenge lies in standardizing the measurement of heat stress exposure. This
challenge is explored in Section 2.6.1, which details existing standards for heat stress
assessment and discusses their utility in communicating heat exposure.
In conclusion, extreme heat exposure is a significant concern, especially in densely popu-
lated urban areas, and its impact varies significantly around the world, largely dependent
on preventative measures taken by public authorities. It is challenging to create univer-
sally applicable definitions due to these variations. The repercussions of such exposure
can range from minor symptoms like fatigue and headaches to severe, life-threatening
consequences. There is a pressing need to raise global awareness and understanding of
these risks and their potential impact.
2.2
Sub-Saharan Africa: a future challenge
As the seriousness of extreme heat events becomes evident worldwide, scientists, Non-
governmental organization (NGO)s, and intergovernmental groups like the WHO and
IPCC are raising the alarm about Africa’s specific vulnerabilities to such exposures [4].
The total population of Africa is projected to rise from 1.3 billion in 2020 to 2.5 billion by
2050 [15]. Similarly, the urban population is expected to increase substantially by an extra
950 million people. Unfortunately, urban development in Africa often happens without
adequate planning and robust policies, exacerbating the risk of heat-related disasters
[16].
Moreover, Africa struggles with additional problems, such as an unstable energy sup-
ply. For example, South Africa is confronting an energy crisis due to economic issues
with the electricity provider, Eskom [17]. Therefore, it seems unfeasible to augment en-
ergy consumption for cooling buildings. Furthermore, South Africa is plagued with sub-
stantial disparities between regions, a legacy of the apartheid era. The populations in
pre-apartheid areas are more vulnerable. These areas are characterised by low-income
and social housing. Studies have pointed out these houses’ susceptibility to high indoor
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
5
==PAGE:20==
temperatures [18]. Similarly, [19] found that during warmer seasons, Reconstruction and
Development Program (RDP) houses and low-income housing, such as shacks, recorded
higher temperatures than other types of houses. In contrast, these houses fail to provide
adequate warmth for comfort in winter. Furthermore, temperatures in low-income and im-
poverished houses are more sensitive to outdoor temperature fluctuations, often leading
to higher indoor temperatures during summer. Comparable problems are found in regions
like Delhi, India, where temperatures in low-income housing can exceed 21 degrees Cel-
sius for over 40% of the time [20]. Additionally, these households often face poor air qual-
ity, primarily caused by indoor firewood burning. [21] emphasised the need to improve the
indoor environmental quality in low-income housing, leading to better socio-economical
development and improved health outcomes.
Aside from urban areas like Johannesburg, rural regions are not spared from heat-related
problems. For instance, economically disadvantaged rural communities, like those in
Giyani in the Limpopo province, are susceptible to high indoor temperatures during the
summer and spring periods [9]. These communities should be a priority for the implemen-
tation of adaptive solutions to prevent heat-related illnesses.
Meanwhile, elderly populations, often residing in social housing in places like Durban, are
also at risk from heat stress and need better guidance and support [22]. Overcrowding,
particularly in impoverished suburbs of Johannesburg, presents an additional challenge,
with 57% of dwellings considered overcrowded based on United Nations (UN) definitions,
leading to health issues like respiratory and gastrointestinal syndromes, and fever [23].
However, compared to other climate change adaptation needs, heat stress and exposure
in Africa’s informal and low-income urban settlements are often overlooked, leading to a
lack of resources in these areas [24].
Heat-related issues also extend to health facilities. For example, many clinics lack tem-
perature monitors in rural Limpopo province. Indoor temperatures at these clinics often
exceed those outdoors, exposing vulnerable visitors to even higher temperatures [25].
In conclusion, low-income housing types like shacks, RDP houses from the post-apartheid
era, and social housing from the pre-apartheid era are more vulnerable to extreme heat
exposure than wealthier areas in South Africa. There is a need to raise policymakers’
awareness of these issues and encourage them to provide or research solutions to help
communities cope with the increasing risks posed by climate change, which will be the
subject of the next section.
2.3
Heat stress, growing risks in the wake of climate change
Global warming is intensifying temperature-related concerns worldwide. The latest report
by the IPCC highlights that the global surface temperature increased by 1.09°C during the
2011-2020 period compared to the reference period of 1850-1900. This increase varied
significantly between land and sea. In particular, the land’s global surface temperature
surged by 1.59°C during the same period.
The report further suggests that 3.3 to 3.6 billion inhabit regions highly vulnerable to cli-
mate change, as highlighted in Section A.2.2 in [4]. The frequency of extreme heat events
has risen considerably, leading to increased human mortality and morbidity in these re-
gions, as pointed out in Section A.2.5 of the report. Such extreme heat events contribute
to mental health challenges such as trauma and even prompt population displacement
in areas like Africa, Asia, and North America, as asserted with high confidence by the
IPCC (A.2.6). However, these events are unevenly distributed across regions and com-
munities. Disparities, particularly regarding gender and social equity, place the poorer
6
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:21==
and female populations at greater risk. The rise in urban temperatures has negatively
impacted human health, causing economic losses, service disruptions, healthcare facil-
ities, and declines in well-being, disproportionately affecting socially marginalised urban
residents such as the poor and women (A.2.7).
According to the IPCC’s Interactive Atlas available online 1, southeastern Africa, with
high confidence, is expected to face increases in surface temperature and extreme heat
periods, along with drought, fire weather, and aridity, under any climate change scenario.
The projections indicate that Africa will likely experience a more significant rise in land
surface temperature than the global average. Figure 2.3 shows the heat stress impact on
humans in outdoor conditions for 3 RCP projections compared with the historical 1991-
2005 heat stress across the globe.
Figure 2.3: Projection IPCC AR6 SYR which shows the impact of heat-stress from tem-
perature and humidity on humans given the 3 RCP projections
Source: [26]
[27] further asserts that global warming will exacerbate existing challenges, with poten-
tially devastating impacts on human health.
Moreover, the development of climate-resilient programs requires enhanced climate ser-
vices. Current services often lack accuracy, are difficult to access, and are experiencing
a decline in station numbers. The success of climate-resilient programs hinges on the
reliability of these services and the commitment of authorities, elements currently lacking
in South Africa [28].
In conclusion, climate change and increased land temperatures due to global warming
are likely to exacerbate existing disparities between richer and poorer housing, as seen
in South Africa.
2.4
Artificial Intelligence: a solution?
Over the past decade, the application of Artificial Intelligence (AI) in indoor environments
and building engineering has significantly developed [29]. Before embarking on an ex-
tensive literature review, it’s crucial to understand the meaning of AI within the scope of
this thesis. AI, as defined by Britannica, is the capability of a computer or a computer-
controlled robot to perform tasks typically associated with human intellect [30]. In this
research, AI is perceived as the process of creating intelligent machines [31]. This thesis
will focus primarily on a subset of AI known as machine learning.
1https://interactive-atlas.ipcc.ch/permalink/mgTzEOwW, accessed on 16-07-2023
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
7
==PAGE:22==
Machine learning, a critical component of AI, could initially seem intricate, but it primarily
deals with using past experiences to train a model for future data handling. Despite the
vast coverage of machine learning, this simplified description is adequate for this thesis’s
purpose. The theoretical background and main definitions of machine learning will be de-
tailed in Section 2.6.2. The following focuses on an overview of recent machine-learning
research in indoor environmental engineering.
Machine learning’s application in predictive modelling is noticeably growing. This includes
using machine learning to estimate or predict energy demand. In this domain, Artificial
Neural Networks (ANN) have shown encouraging results in forecasting heating and cool-
ing electricity consumption, albeit with limitations in broadening these results to different
building types [32]. Deep neural networks have also been effectively used to predict ther-
mal comfort based on indoor air temperature and humidity, subsequently used to control
the HVAC system [33]. This controller was promising for optimizing energy consumption
while maintaining optimal thermal comfort.
ANN, a computational model inspired by networks of biological neurons, wherein the neu-
rons compute output values from inputs [34], have shown potential in predicting thermal
comfort using Thermal Comfort Vote and Thermal Sensation Vote in buildings [35]. Sim-
ilarly, machine learning models have demonstrated better thermal comfort predictions
than traditional models, like the Predicted Mean Vote (PMV) [36]. This enables user-
driven HVAC control, which bases the air temperature set point on occupants’ thermal
preferences. Machine learning-based comfort models have shown promising potential in
representing thermal comfort in buildings [37].
On the other hand, Random Forest models, a combination of tree predictors such that
each tree depends on the values of a random vector sampled independently and with the
same distribution for all trees in the forest [38] and [39], have demonstrated high accuracy
in predicting indoor temperatures, particularly in sub-Saharan buildings [40]. Machine
learning models outperform BES due to benefits such as fewer input parameters, less
understanding of building physics, and faster simulation times for energy consumption
prediction [41]. Moreover, ensemble models - a combination of different machine learning
models - have shown promising results [41].
In the field of indoor temperature and relative humidity prediction, prerequisites for al-
most all thermal comfort models, the Neural Network auto-regressive with external in-
put (NNARX) model (learn to predict one time series given past values of the same
time series, the feedback input and another time series called the external time series
[42]) combined with a genetic algorithm (method for solving both constrained and uncon-
strained optimization problems based on a natural selection process that mimics biologi-
cal evolution [43]), has been effective [44]. This model has the advantage of having been
tested in an actual building in Brazil and showed an effective response.
With regard to climate change and its impact on thermal comfort, some propose using
machine learning to predict future heat stress scenarios in light of the increasing heat-
related health effects and mortality [8] [27].
Additionally, machine learning models can be used for superior energy simulations and
better modelling of occupant behaviour, as suggested by Bonte [45]. BES coupled with
mathematical functions based on a trial-error mechanism can better predict energy con-
sumption based on occupant behaviour in terms of set points and clothing insulation.
Finally, there have been attempts to use machine learning to predict energy demand in
low-income housing in Greece, aiming to improve thermal performance and comfort [46].
8
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:23==
The same study showed the potential of k-means clustering on time series of building en-
ergy consumption and showed a relation between clusters and socio-economical indica-
tors of households. Similar work has been conducted to predict the thermal and electrical
energy demands of rural healthcare centres in Africa, to enhance their energy supply and
reliability [47].
In conclusion, the application of machine learning in buildings is of growing interest, pri-
marily in energy demand prediction and is studied mainly in developed countries for user
comfort-driven building controls. The primary role of machine learning is to improve en-
ergy efficiency. However, due to climate change and increasing heat-related risks, ma-
chine learning models need to be implemented to predict thermal comfort for prevention
and communication over low-income housing in Africa [8]. Although similar applications
have been explored in Greek low-income housing, [48] and indoor temperature predic-
tion in Africa [40], more research is needed on the application of straightforward machine
learning models designed for EAS for heat stress in Africa. This will be the focus of this
master’s thesis.
2.5
Exploring Complementary Solutions to Combat Heat
Stress
The previous section discussed the potential of AI, specifically machine learning, as a
vital tool in managing the escalating issue of heat stress in Africa. This would ideally
take the form of an EAS, acting as a preventive measure in cooperation with public au-
thorities. However, it must be pondered if this is the sole solution to counter heat stress
within buildings. This section briefly glimpses the body of literature presenting various
solutions to mitigate heat stress within structures. It’s abundantly clear that addressing
such a formidable challenge would require a two-pronged approach: an effective EAS for
prevention and physical modifications to the buildings to minimise risk. Given the sub-
stantial investment required for the latter, pursuing this avenue alone is not feasible in the
short term.
Research conducted in climatically similar regions, such as Thailand, suggests that en-
hancing thermal insulation, implementing improved shading devices, and adopting noc-
turnal window opening habits can help avoid heat gains during the day and facilitate heat
release through nocturnal cooling. Daylight must be efficiently managed with shading de-
vices to maintain natural light sources during the daytime. Particularly, roof insulation has
proven effective, especially when combined with corrugated steel sheets, which reduce
radiation from the metal sheet to the indoor environment [49]. While these recommen-
dations are specific to Thai school facilities, similar approaches should apply to Southern
Africa, given its hot and humid climatic conditions.
Simultaneously, natural ventilation is worth exploring to mitigate heat-related health risks.
Yet, the effectiveness of this method during heatwaves relies heavily on a comprehen-
sive understanding of building characteristics, local climatic peculiarities, and appropriate
occupant behaviour. There is a degree of uncertainty and risk with this strategy, as mis-
understanding the right times to open windows could intensify heat risks [50].
Interestingly, within the African context, the use of earthbag buildings and increased ther-
mal inertia have significantly improved thermal comfort. The indoor temperature remains
relatively stable and cooler during the hottest hours of the day while providing warmth
during the cooler night hours, courtesy of the unique thermal dynamics [51].
It’s worth noting that passive design strategies, such as shading, natural ventilation, and
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
9
==PAGE:24==
thermal mass, can reduce indoor temperatures by up to 4 degrees Celsius, significantly
improving occupants’ thermal comfort and resilience by reducing reliance on costly me-
chanical cooling devices, as demonstrated in a study in Ghana [52]. However, these pas-
sive design solutions should be prioritized, especially considering that local, low-income
African residents often lack the means to adapt their homes to heat. They lack an under-
standing of how building dynamics affect heat and the necessary resources. Despite the
risks to the population from heat stress, public authorities need to lead prevention pro-
grams that explain key concepts, as well as invest in necessary infrastructure or devices
like street shading. Financial constraints may slow implementation.[53]
In conclusion, a machine learning-based prevention system could be an integral part of
a broader prevention program. This would involve increasing population awareness and
action towards housing adaptations, providing solutions like shading and roof insulation
at lower costs, and promoting increased thermal mass and controlled natural ventilation.
2.5.1
Reference article
This section deepens into two studies that employ an epidemiological approach to as-
sess heat stress in two distinct regions of South Africa: Johannesburg and Giyani in the
Limpopo Province. The first study centres on low-income urban areas, while the second
highlights the risks in low-income rural areas. These studies are frequently cited through-
out this thesis, as the current work is founded on the data shared by Caradee Wright from
the South African Medical Research Council (SAMRC), which was utilized in both studies.
A summary of the context of both studies is necessary to comprehend the backdrop of
the current research.
2.5.1.1
Low-income housing in Johannesburg
In urban development and public health, understanding the living conditions in low-cost
housing is crucial. A study by Naicker et al. (2016) [18] offers valuable findings regarding
indoor temperatures in such residences in Johannesburg, South Africa, and their subse-
quent health implications for inhabitants. Researchers undertook a cross-sectional study
across five low-income areas in Johannesburg (Table 2.1), using data loggers for record-
ing hourly measurements of indoor temperatures and relative humidity.

Findings from this study unearthed significant discrepancies in indoor temperatures across

However, this study isn’t without its limitations. The small sample size could have im-

searchers acknowledged that temperature variability might have been more pronounced
during extreme weather periods. Also, the use of a single temperature data logger in each
dwelling’s living room may not have fully captured the variability between the houses.
In conclusion, the study by Naicker et al. [18] emphasizes the role of housing design and
construction in shaping living conditions in low-cost housing. It underlines the necessity
for more extensive research and policy interventions to enhance thermal efficiency in such
dwellings, corroborating suggestions from previous studies.
2.5.1.2
Southern Africa rural housing in Giyani, Limpopo Province
The paper written by Kapwata et al. [9] provides a comprehensive analysis of the impacts
of climate change on housing, particularly in the context of rural areas in the Limpopo
province of South Africa.
The study was conducted in 406 households in the Greater Giyani municipality in Limpopo
province. The province has a high level of poverty and is particularly vulnerable to climate
change impacts due to its exposure to extreme weather events, particularly extreme tem-
peratures.
The methodology involved monitoring and recording temperatures and relative humidity
inside the houses using LogTag temperature loggers. Three separate sampling cam-
paigns were conducted, one for each season (spring, summer, and winter). For the spring
and summer campaigns, one logger was placed in a room in each of the selected 406
dwellings. Temperature and relative humidity were recorded at one-hour intervals during
spring and summer and at 2-hour intervals during the winter campaign in a sub-sample
of 98 houses.
The study found that the indoor temperatures in most sampled houses were above the
South African Weather Services (SAWS) defined comfort zone of 18–24 °C for more than
5 hours of the day during summer (21% of the time). The study also projected that by
the end of the century, under a high emission scenario, the number of hours that indoor
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
13
==PAGE:28==
Figure 2.6: Houses in study site, Giyani, Limpopo province in South Africa
Source: [9]
temperatures would be above the upper limit of the comfort zone would increase by 2–4
hours (corresponding to 30 to 38% of the time outside the comfort zone).
The study also found that the type of roof significantly impacted indoor temperatures.
Houses with metal roofs were hotter than those with thatched roofs during summer. The
researchers suggested that painting the roofs white or covering them with a reflective
sheet could help reduce indoor temperatures as well as using insulation. Figure 2.6 shows
the house types studied.
However, the study had some limitations. The study was conducted in a single province,
and the findings may not be generalizable to other regions in South Africa and largely in
Sub-Saharan Africa. Additionally, the study did not consider the potential impacts of other
climate change-related factors, for example, such as changes in precipitation patterns
resulting in flooding. The study also did not consider the potential adaptive capacity of the
households or the potential impacts of future changes in building materials and designs.
2.5.2
Conclusion
Looking at past research shows how urgent it is to deal with heat stress in vulnerable areas
like Sub-Saharan Africa. New studies and reports on climate change underline the need
for prevention and good communication to handle heat stress. Early warnings about heat
waves and smart preventive strategies from public authorities are key to reducing deaths
caused by heat stress. With global warming causing more and more extreme heat events,
these actions are urged to be implemented.
Sub-Saharan Africa stands out as a region with particularly high vulnerability. It not only
faces the likelihood of significant ambient temperature increase but also tussles with eco-
nomic constraints, being generally poorer than other regions of the world. In this context,
low-income housing emerges as an area of critical concern.
Simultaneously, machine learning has demonstrated considerable potential in predicting
building-related performance indicators, often surpassing BES regarding input simplicity,
knowledge requirements, and computational needs. In light of these factors, the thesis’s
aim, which is to use artificial intelligence to predict indoor temperature to improve thermal
comfort in buildings in Sub-Saharan Africa subjected to climate change, is substantiated.

2.6
Theory
This Section focuses on the theory underlying this master’s thesis. It does not aim to
cover all the theory that exists within the two topics (heat stress and machine learning)
but to settle the key definitions and explanations necessary to follow the work done in this
thesis.
2.6.1
Heat stress
To assess the heat exposure on human health, there are different methods available as
standards, such as ISO 7933([54]) and ISO 7243 ([55]). These standards generally de-
scribe two methods to calculate heat exposure with two different degrees of complexity.
One uses the Wet Bulb Globe Temperature, i.e. Wet Bulb Globe Temperature (WBGT),
and the second uses the calculation of the Predicted Heat Strain (PHS) using empirical
correlations from experimental studies. Aside from the two standards, the apparent tem-
perature seems to be widely used in literature due to its simplicity [18] and [9]. However,
from PHS to Apparent Temperature (AT), information is lost and PHS is certainly the best
model to assess heat stress due to the multiple indicators calculated but also the most
complex while AT is less accurate, for example, it does not take into account the clothing
insulation either the duration of exposure. However, it is easier to implement because it
requires only a few measurements, air temperature, relative humidity and wind speed.
2.6.1.1
Predicted Heat Strain, PHS
The PHS index is the object of the ISO standards [54] is a complex method used to assess
heat stress in various environments. It is based on a human heat balance equation as
follows:l
M −W = Cres + Eres + K + C + R + E + S
(2.1)
This equation determines the internal heat production of the body corresponding to the
subtraction of the metabolic rate (M) from the effective mechanical power (W). This left
side of the equation is balanced to the respiratory heat transfer, i.e. convection (Cres)
and evaporation (Eres), and the skin heat transfers, i.e. conduction (K), convection (C),
radiation (R), and evaporation (E), and finally the heat storage in the body (S). As this
standard is not focusing on heat transfer by contact with a solid body, the conduction (K)
is neglected.
The PHS index is calculated using a series of equations that account for these factors. The
method begins with the calculation of the required sweat rate Swreq, which is the amount
of sweat a person needs to produce to maintain thermal equilibrium. This is based on the
required evaporative heat flow Ereq but corrected by the sweat that trickles away due to
variation in local skin wettedness as shown in Equation 2.2.
Swreq = Ereq
rreq
(2.2)
In parallel, the skin wettedness wreq is defined as the ratio between the required evap-
orative heat flow and the maximum evaporative heat flow at the skin surface given by
Equation 2.3.
wreq = Ereq
Emax
(2.3)
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
15
==PAGE:30==
The method then calculates the maximum sweat rate and maximum skin wettedness that
the body can physiologically achieve, considering factors such as the individual’s acclima-
tization state and clothing evaporative resistance. If the required sweat rate exceeds the
maximum sweat rate, the method predicts a rise in body temperature. If the required skin
wettedness is higher than the maximum skin wettedness, the method predicts an increase
in body temperature. Body temperature is measured as rectal temperature. This temper-
ature can achieve a maximum value which prevents any pathological effect, tre,max. Next
to this maximum value, another strain criterion is fixed: the water loss. It is restricted to
a value, Dmax that assures maintenance of the hydromineral equilibrium. From the max
rectal temperature and the max water losses, the maximum allowable exposure time can
be computed, Dlim. All these values are computed given empirical expressions defined
in labs.
Moreover, the PHS method also calculates the sweat efficiency, which is the proportion
of the sweat that can evaporate. If the sweat efficiency is less than 1, it means that not
all the sweat can evaporate, leading to a wet skin surface. The method then predicts the
increase in core and skin temperatures, as well as the sweat rate and dehydration over
time.
However, it has certain limitations. The method is considered valid within specific ranges
of conditions. For example, it is valid for air temperatures between 15 and 50 degrees
Celsius, air velocities up to 3 meters per second, and metabolic rates between 100 and
450 Watts. When one or more parameters are outside this range, the method should be
used with caution.
In conclusion, the PHS index is a comprehensive tool for assessing heat stress, despite
limitations inherent to the sample of subjects used to determine empirical equations. The
PHS is complex to determine due to the extensive amount of parameters needed to calcu-
late the required indicators and strain indicators; for example, it needs clothing insulation,
metabolic rate, atmospheric pressure, and more, which are often unavailable. In the con-
text of low-income housing heat stress prediction, it appears unrealistic to have access to
all these parameters at once. Therefore, even if it is mentioned for the completeness of
the present work, this method is no longer going to be used in further analysis.
2.6.1.2
Wet Bulb Globe Temperature, WBGT
The WBGT is a compound temperature used to estimate the effect of temperature, humid-
ity, wind speed, and solar radiation on humans. It is a heat stress index that measures the
overall thermal environment to which an individual or population is exposed. The WBGT
has been widely used in various industries, including construction, to assess the risk of
heat-related illnesses and to guide operational procedures, particularly in Qatar during
the construction of stadiums for the Football World Cup [11].
The WBGT is calculated using the following equations:
WBGT (outdoor with solar load) = 0.7 × Tnw + 0.2 × Tg + 0.1 × Ta
(2.4)
WBGT (indoor or outdoor without solar load) = 0.7 × Tnw + 0.3 × Tg
(2.5)
where: Tnw is the natural wet bulb temperature,
Tg is the globe temperature, and
Ta is the air temperature.
16
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:31==
Figure 2.7: WBGT sensor used to measure instant WBGT
Source: https://www.dvidshub.net/image/491824
The natural wet bulb temperature (Tnw) is a measure of temperature obtained by covering
a temperature sensor with a wetted wick that is ventilated naturally. The globe temper-
ature (Tg) represents the combined effects of radiation and convection, measured inside
a black globe. The air temperature (Ta) is the actual dry bulb air temperature. The natu-
ral wet bulb temperature estimation can be measured directly or indirectly despite higher
complexity and less reliability. In practice, WBGT can be calculated using a sensor that
can be bought on the market as shown in Figure 2.7.
The WBGT index does not consider the clothing worn by the individual or the individ-
ual’s metabolic rate, both of which can significantly affect heat stress. However, clothing
adjustment values (CAVs) in WBGT temperature units are provided for different clothing
materials and configurations. The CAV is added to the measured WBGT to produce an
effective WBGT. The metabolic rate is also a significant contributor to heat stress. A valid
estimate of this is essential for the assessment [55]. Once all parameters are known,
a conversion table using metabolic rate provides WBGT effective for an acclimatized or
unacclimatized person to heat. For example, for a low metabolic rate, and acclimatized
person, the WBGT effective limit is 30 deg Celsius.
In the context of this thesis, the measurement of black globe and natural wet bulb temper-
atures has not been provided, presenting a challenge in evaluating these parameters due
to data scarcity (no radiation data). Consequently, the WBGT will not be utilized in this
study. Nonetheless, this heat stress index harbours significant potential for application
and thus warrants further investigation in future studies.
2.6.1.3
Apparent Temperature, AT
In addition to PHS and WBGT, Apparent Temperature is a straightforward and effective
index for assessing heat stress, exhibiting a strong correlation with relative humidity and
air temperature. However, it does have a notable limitation in that it does not account for
the radiation component, unlike the previously mentioned indices. Apparent Temperature
amalgamates air temperature and absolute air humidity to estimate a ”real-feel” temper-
ature.
The calculation of Apparent Temperature is based on the following formula:
AT = Ta + 0.33 × e −0.70 × ws −4.00
(2.6)
The water vapour pressure (e) is calculated using Equation 2.7 from relative humidity,
which is then used in Equation 2.6. The wind speed (ws) is typically disregarded in indoor
environments [9].
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
17
==PAGE:32==
e = RH/100 × 6.105 × exp 17.27 × Ta
237.7 + Ta
(2.7)
The concept of Apparent Temperature has been employed as an indicator of thermal
comfort and sensation in several studies [18, 9]. To facilitate a comparison between the
analyses in this work and those in the aforementioned studies, the use of AT has been
adopted.
However, AT requires classification into categories, referred to as heat stress categories
in this work. These categories are defined using predetermined ranges. The following
categorization is applied in this study:

By categorizing the AT into heat stress categories, a valuable metric is provided for as-
sessing and analyzing the level of heat stress experienced in various scenarios or envi-
ronments. This index requires a few parameters. More accurate and detailed heat stress
indices such as WBGT and PHS would have been preferable, but the necessary input
parameters for their calculation were unavailable. As a result, only the AT was feasible.
It should be noted that the categorization used here is derived from the US NWS NOAA
heat index symptom bands described in [9]. However, a standard limitation among WBGT
and AT is the lack of consideration for the duration of exposure to these categories.
2.6.2
Machine Learning
In Sections 2.6.2.5 and 2.6.2.4, respectively, supervised and unsupervised learning theory
is covered only for the purpose of this master’s thesis. It is not exhaustive. Therefore,
only the necessary concepts and definitions will be covered. This Section is primarily
done using the reference book from the course 02450 Introduction to machine learning
and data mining, taught at the Technical University of Denmark [31]. Parts that are from
other sources will be identified. Otherwise, it is assumed to be inspired by the course.
2.6.2.1
Machine learning in general
Machine learning here is mainly used by training a dataset of the form X from which
the output is known (supervised learning), i.e. y, or is unknown, called unsupervised
learning. The latter will be used using clustering methods and is described in Section
2.6.2.4 while the supervised learning is the focus of Section 2.6.2.5 and includes only
classification methods, prediction of a categorical output. In contrast to classification,
regression learning is disregarded in the present work since it is not addressed in this
18
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:33==
work. Machine learning aims to define the prediction rule f trained over a sub-sample
Dtrain = (X, y). After defining f, y is predicted given the following:
yi = f(xi)
(2.8)
The data set was split into training and testing data sets which the union represented all
the available data.
2.6.2.2
Data Pre-processing
Standard Scaling was used in the data processing. It is often used to standardize the
features by removing the mean and scaling to unit variance. This feature transformation
is often used to reduce the impact of outliers and different scales among the features.
Standard Scaling has been applied to continuous features in the present work.
2.6.2.3
Principal Component Analysis
Principle Component Analysis (PCA) is a projection technique widely used in the field
of machine learning as a tool to reduce the dimensions of a data set. It identifies the
axes (principal components) in the feature space along which the data varies the most.
The data is then projected onto these axes, resulting in a new set of features that are
uncorrelated with each other. In the context of time series data, PCA can identify the
main patterns in the multivariate over time, which is used in the present work to reduce
multivariate time series (indoor temperature, relative humidity and weather data) into the
first two principal components for each time step. Then, PCA is also used for projecting
time series into a two-dimensional plane for assessing the clustering method. Figure 2.8
explains graphically the projection along principal components PC1 and PC2 and shows
the results of the projection.
Figure 2.8: Principal Component Analysis projection from original space to PCA reduced
space
Source: https://towardsdatascience.com/pca-clearly-explained-how-when-why-to-use-it-and-
feature-importance-a-guide-in-python-7c274582c37e
2.6.2.4
Unsupervised learning and data mining
Unsupervised learning refers to the class of machine learning tasks where the model is
trained on a dataset without labelled responses (y is unknown). The goal is to identify pat-
terns, clusters, or associations in the data. The fundamental technique used in this work
is time series K-means clustering. K-means clustering is a method that partitions the data
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
19
==PAGE:34==
into K distinct, non-overlapping clusters based on their distances to the K centroids. The
k-means uses Dynamic Time Warping (DTW) as a distance in the present work instead
of the classic Euclidean distance. DTW often is used with time series of different lengths
to calculate the distance between time series. DTW is widely used in speech recognition.
However, here, it was used to compare similar patterns that have similar patterns but
scaled differently. [56]
The K-means algorithm is first initialized with random locations for the centroids. Every
sample is assigned to the nearest centroid, updated of the centroid as the mean of the
points assigned to it, and repeated until the location converges to a specific location.
2.6.2.5
Supervised learning
Supervised learning is a type of machine learning where the model is trained on a dataset
that includes both the input features and the corresponding target outputs (X, y). Based
on this training data, the model learns to map the input to the output. Random Forest
and Bagging are two ensemble methods used in supervised learning and are used in
this present work. Random Forest involves training multiple decision trees on different
subsets of the data and averaging their predictions. Bagging, or Bootstrap Aggregating,
is a similar technique that reduces variance and helps to avoid overfitting.
Bagging is an ensemble method that aims to reduce the variance of a machine-learning
model. It begins with dataset D of size N. It then randomly selects T new datasets D...DT
of size N0 ≤N by randomly sub-sampling D. The same classification model is then trained
on each T dataset to produce T different classifiers, which are then combined into a single
classifier. For example, the number of classifiers T can be selected using cross-validation
or as a high number. The final prediction is made by majority voting of the output. Figure
2.9 shows the principles of the Bagging algorithm.
Random Forests is an extension of bagging applied to decision trees. The method creates
a set of decision trees from a randomly selected subset of the training set. It also alters
the algorithm for how the splits for each node of the decision tree are chosen. Instead
of considering every feature, it only considers a subset of features selected randomly for
each new tree node applying Hunt’s algorithm 2. Therefore, since not all the same features
are available at each node, it does not provide correlated trees. The final prediction is
made by majority voting of the output. This method is effective because it reduces the
variance without increasing the bias. With Random Forest, the feature importance can be
measured based on the average reduction in impurity due to splits on a particular feature
across all trees. Figure 2.10 shows the principles of the Random Forest Classifier.
2.6.2.6
Performance evaluation
Performance evaluation involves assessing how well the trained model performs on un-
seen data, called testing data. k-fold cross-validation is a technique used in this work
for this purpose, where the data is split into ’k’ subsets and the model is trained on ’k-1’
subsets and tested on the remaining subset. This process is repeated ’k’ times so that
each subset is used for testing once. This helps to give a more robust measure of model
performance. Besides the use of performance metrics described in the following Section,
the training computation time is also used to evaluate the performance of a model.
2.6.2.7
Performance metrics
Performance metrics are used to quantify the performance of a machine learning model.
For classification tasks, standard metrics include Accuracy, the proportion of correct pre-
dictions out of total predictions, and the F1 score, which is the harmonic mean of precision
2Algorithm used in Decision Trees to consider many potential splits and select the best split based on
purity gain

Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:35==

==PAGE:36==
and recall. The confusion matrix is a table that describes the performance of a classifica-
tion model by showing a matrix showing predicted categories against actual, true, cate-
gories.
Accuracy is therefore computed as the sum of the diagonal of the confusion matrix divided
by the total number of predictions. Accuracy is very sensitive to overfitting. Accuracy is
also very sensitive to class imbalance, which occurs when one category occurs exces-
sively compared to others. Therefore Recall and Precision need to be computed.
The recall is equal to the correctly predicted categories divided by the number of instances
actually in that category when the precision is equal to the correctly predicted categories
divided by all the correctly predicted categories.
The F1-score is defined as:
F1 = 2 × precision × recall
precision + recall
(2.9)
2.6.2.8
Mutual Information
Mutual Information, on the other hand, is a way to quantify how dependent two variables
are. Mutual information of 0 means that the two variables are independent. The higher
the mutual information, the more the two variables depend on each other.

Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:37==
3
Methodology
The methodology described in the following can be summarized as shown in Figure 3.1.
This work structure follows a classical machine learning workflow which consists of collect-
ing data, processing data, training the models, evaluating the models, tuning the model
and then deploying the model and getting predictions from the model. In the present work,
the last two steps will not be covered. The deployment of the model and its use is the
focus of further studies. Two branches are explored, the measurement data (orange) and
the BES data (purple) are investigated in parallel. Following, an experimental approach is
added to the workflow focusing on data mining techniques. ”Data mining is the discovery
of patterns and relationships in data and translating these into a useful structure” [31].
This experimental part is highlighted in blue in the flowchart and aims to enable the full
potential of on-site measurement data. After having explored the potential of data mining
to label measurement data with building characteristics, the machine learning workflow is
continued with the selection of the model, its fine-tuning and its evaluation presented in the
green. These 4 colours follow the structure of the methodology. Each section presents a
more detailed flowchart which specifies the different steps.
3.1
Measurements Data
Figure 3.2 shows the flowchart of the methodology used to treat the data from the on-site
measurements.
3.1.1
Data Collection
Data were collected from two studies in South Africa. One was held in Johannesburg
and its neighbourhood, and the other was held in the province of Limpopo, in Giyani City.
These data sets represent two different scenarios: an urban and a rural setting. The
data were acquired from Caradee Wright from the SAMRC 1. A confidentiality agreement
protects the data. The data from Johannesburg referred to [18] and Giyani [9]. Both
studies are summarized in Section 2.5.1.
The Johannesburg data included 79 buildings in which the indoor air temperature and the
indoor relative humidity were logged for a period fluctuating between 16 January 2014
and 22 July 2014, corresponding to summer and autumn. All the data were collected
using ”Logtag HAXO-8” operated on batteries as shown in Figure 3.3. These loggers
continuously measured both parameters with a 1-hour time increment [18]. The SAMRC
supplied and tabulated the data in Excel format. Each sheet represents one location’s
time-series (TS) of indoor air temperature and indoor relative humidity. None of the loca-
tions covered identical periods because of the slightly different timing of installation and
recollection of data loggers.
The Giyani data were collected using LogTag temperature loggers inside 406 dwellings.
As described in [9]: ”Temperature and relative humidity were recorded at one-hour inter-
vals during spring (5 September 2016 to 30 November 2016) and summer (1 December
2016 to 28 February 2017). The same variables were recorded at 2-h intervals during the
winter campaign (7 July 2017 to 31 August 2017) in a sub-sample of 98. [...]. The logger
was placed in a room frequently used by household occupants and affixed to a wall.” The

The SAWS supplied weather data. The data set included outdoor dry bulb tempera-

3.1.2
Data Processing
For the data processing, it was chosen to use Python 3.10.9 in Visual Studio Code 1.79.2
and using Anaconda 2022.10. The data are stored locally on the computer per the Data
Acquisition Agreement signed with the SAMRC. The processing used Python libraries
such as pandas, numpy, matplotlib and seaborn to process and visualise the data. Ap-
pendix A.2.2 shows Table A.8 with the Python libraries’ version used in the present work.
At first, the Johannesburg data were imported under TS tables (Pandas DataFrame) with
Date Time as an index, enabling date time manipulations.
Then, verification of the dataset quality was performed. Thus, missing values, data types
and conformity of the data types to the attributes were verified. Every household’s record
was analyzed, and the ones that were not complete during the joint date range or those
with more than 5% missing values or incorrect data needed to be removed. The records
showing less than 5% of missing values were kept, and internal interpolation was com-
puted to fill the missing values. These aforementioned operations made the data set
complete and conformed for future data manipulation.
Consequently, the Johannesburg households’ indoor air temperature and relative humid-
ity were merged with the weather data. Hence, the data set was merged with the closest
date-time value of the household’s records.
To go further, the data set did not include any information on the building characteristics
and occupant behaviour and data. The data exists in the reference article [18]. However,
these data were not shared between the parties for ethical purposes. Therefore, a solu-
tion was adopted to perform data mining techniques to classify the different households’
records into groups of similar patterns. Section 3.3.1 describes this latter’s methodology.
2Precipitable water is defined as ”The total atmospheric water vapour contained in a vertical column of the
unit cross-sectional area extending between any two specified levels, commonly expressed in terms of the
height to which that water substance would stand if completely condensed and collected in a vessel of the
same unit cross-section.” [57] The term was mistaken for water precipitation in the present work. However,
the precipitation is not characterizing the southern African climate as such as the confusion has not been
leveraged. Consequently, when the term refers to measurements it is understood as precipitation and when
referring to EPW files, as precipitable water.

Similarly to Johannesburg households’ data, Giyani data were manipulated to assess the

for indoor air temperature and relative humidity. Similarly to Johannesburg households’
data set, households’ records presenting more than 5% missing values or Not a Num-
ber (NaN) values were carefully removed, and the households’ records presenting fewer

3.2
Building Energy Simulation, BES
Figure 3.4 shows the flowchart specific to the BES. All the steps are the depicted in this
section.
3.2.1
Simulations (Data Collection)
BES provides detailed insights into the energy performance and thermal comfort condi-
tions within buildings under different weather scenarios. It served as a cornerstone of
the study, enabling us to evaluate the influence of building characteristics and occupant
behaviour features in the classification task. BES was mainly used to assess thermal
comfort and generate a parametric run of a simple model of two building scenarios devel-
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
27
==PAGE:42==
oped in the reference studies [18] and [9]. The two studied simulation model types were
shacks (or informal housing), located primarily in the district Hospital Hill and the RDP
houses in the Braamfischerville district for [18] and also present in Giyani.
On the other hand, the simulations were used to leverage the lack of building and occu-
pant characteristics in the measurement data. Hence, BES allowed to perform features
aggregation and sensitivity analysis of the different inputs to predict the heat stress. The
relation between the measurements and the simulations data will be discussed in Section
3.3.1.
In the context of this study, the simulations model was used in the current and future
climate scenarios. Section 3.2.1.2 outlines how BES was used to model and assess the
present-day climate, laying the groundwork for further analysis in Section 3.3.
3.2.1.1
Model Description
The BES model used in this study hinges on a simplified box model to support rapid
computation and enable the generation of a high number of simulations as described
in Figure 3.5. This model was developed using OpenStudio Software Development Kit
(SDK) Bindings for Python and was later on exported into an Input Data File (IDF) model
format to run an Energy Plus Simulation using OpenStudio Command Line Program (CLI)
and Energy Plus.
The model consisted of a single-zone box representing a simplified version of a Shack
and RDP house. The box was divided into a floor in contact with the ground, a roof and
four wall surfaces. These exterior surfaces were given a window-to-wall ratio, and a door
was placed centred at 0.1 m above ground on a randomly chosen wall surface. Its design
took into consideration several parameters: the floor area (width and length) and building
height (Figure 3.5a), construction sets for the different surfaces types (Figure 3.5b), door
dimensions, the U-factor and Solar Heat Gain Coefficient (SHGC) of the window (Figure
3.5c), the number of occupants and their activity levels, equipment type and their power,
lighting specifications as numbers of incandescent light bulbs, the power of an electric coil
heater (Figure 3.5d), air infiltration rate (Figure 3.5e), and building orientation. Table 3.4
shows the detailed input parameters for shacks (the input parameters for RDP houses
are given in Appendix A.3) and Tables 3.1, 3.2 and 3.3 give the floor, wall and roof sets
compositions respectively. In Appendix A.1.1, Tables A.3, A.4, A.5, and A.6 gives the
values for the RDP houses and Table A.7 gives more details about the characteristics
encoded for each layer.
A set of values was generated using available values from [18] and [9]. Some values were
assumed from pictures in the same literature, and some results of total assumptions, i.e.
the infiltration rate is set to represent a higher or lower building tightness between RDP
houses and shacks.

3.2.1.2
Weather files
For this study, there is the option of using two different types of weather climate files:
the International Weather for Energy Calculations (IWEC) and the Typical Meteorological
Year (TMY). The IWEC weather files, developed under an American Society of Heat-
ing, Refrigerating and Air-Conditioning Engineers (ASHRAE) research project, are widely
used in BES. They encompass a substantial time span, from 12 to 25 years, reflecting bet-
ter the occurrence of extreme temperatures. Conversely, TMY is a climate data method
based on a ’median’ month for each month within a decade, another approach often uti-
lized in BES and developed by the National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL).[58]
The availability of climate data primarily influenced the choice between these two meth-
ods. TMY was the only climate data file available for the Botanical Gardens in Johannes-
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
31
==PAGE:46==
burg. This allowed consistency for further comparison with the household data referring to
the same weather station. Unfortunately, no weather files were available for the location
of Giyani in Limpopo Province.
The organization Climate OneBuilding [59] provides three different TMY files for the Jo-
hannesburg Botanical Gardens 683610:
• Covering the period from 2004 to 2018
• Covering the period from 2007 to 2021
• Covering the period from 2004 to 2021
Out of these options, only the climate data for the period 2004 to 2018 was convertible
into two predicted climate data sets. Thus, this weather file opted for the scope of future
research and developments.
To cover the research with a sense of climate changes and use it for better predictions in
the future, the parametric BES method described in Section 3.2.1.1 is employed with pro-
jecting future climate scenarios. These projections are covered by two weather data sets,
specially curated for Johannesburg, based on the IPCC’s anticipated scenarios for the
years 2050 and 2080 developed in the Third Assessment Report (TAR), formatted in En-
ergy Plus Weather file (EPW), was morphed by the CCWorldWeatherGen tool, a product
of the University of Southampton. This software utilizes summary data from the IPCC’s
TAR, specifically the Hadley Centre’s Climate Model version 3 (HadCM3) A2 scenario.
The tool requires a ’present-day’ climate file, formatted in EPW style, in conjunction with
the HadCM3 data. For our study, the ’present-day’ EPW used was the one referenced in
our current climate scenario analysis (see Section 3.2.1.2). Simultaneously, the neces-
sary HadCM3 data was accessed directly from the IPCC’s comprehensive data collection
[60].
Then, using CCWordlWeatherGen with the retrieved EPW and HadCM3 projection data
allowed the creation of a projected EPW weather file for two different projections: 2050
and 2080. These scenarios enabled us to perform predictive energy simulations that
provide invaluable insights into potential future thermal comfort patterns under projected
climatic conditions.
Note that IPCC newest reports, Sixth Assessment, described newer projection models so-
called RCP, which describe different emission patterns depending on socio-demographic
and geopolitical evolution. This newest model has not been used due to its complexity.
Given that climate change impact is a peripheral scope of this project, it has been chosen
to not further study this part. However, the precedent predictive model suggests insights
for the evolution of indoor thermal comfort under climate with increasing mean ambient
land-level temperature.
3.2.2
Data Processing
The data processing stage involved processing simulation output files in a manner con-
sistent with the methods used for measuring data. The output parameters from the sim-
ulation, namely Indoor Mean Air Temperature and Indoor Relative Humidity, were sub-
sequently combined with several climate parameters. These included Outdoor Dry Bulb
Temperature, Outdoor Relative Humidity, Atmospheric Pressure, Precipitable Water, Wind
Direction, and Wind Speed.
Additionally, a separate process was set in motion to label the simulation output and sim-
ulation input parameters, a necessary step to ensure accurate correspondence between
32
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:47==
Figure 3.6: Flowchart of the feature engineering and aggregation
the different data sets. Labelling the data effectively helped maintain the organization and
integrity of the data sets, thereby aiding subsequent analysis.
3.3
Feature Engineering and Aggregation
This section delves into a comprehensive exploration of simulation results and input pa-
rameters. The initial focus is on the potential of data mining, specifically TS clustering, to
evaluate the relationships between varied simulated model outcomes and the measured
household records. Both k-means clustering and hierarchical clustering methods were uti-
lized to cluster different results simulated under the contemporary climate for shacks and
RDP houses combined in a single dataset. For this clustering process, input parameters
were not provided. The clustering intended to evaluate its potential use with the measure-
ment of household data to ascertain if the labelling of the records reflected significance in
terms of building characteristics. Consequently, only indoor conditions (Indoor Mean Air
Temperature and Relative Humidity) and outdoor weather conditions were offered corre-
sponding to the available data from the measurements (Refer to Section 3.3.1).
Lastly, a machine learning model was trained using the summer period data (December
to March), trained on various feature sets from the raw BES input parameters to simplified
parameters which mirror what a user would realistically input into a mobile application in
the context of EAS (See Section 3.3.3).
3.3.1
The Potential of Data Mining for Data Augmentation
The aim was to use the simulation to enhance the data of the household records and as-
sign to each record a set of building and occupant characteristics generated by clustering
and comparing simulation data and measurement data.
This part aims to gauge the potential of data mining and specifically, clustering methods,
to categorize buildings based on their indoor conditions (Indoor Mean Air Temperature
and Relative Humidity) and outdoor conditions (Dry bulb temperature, Relative Humid-
ity, Precipitable Water, Atmospheric Pressure, Wind Speed, and Direction). Even though
more features are accessible during BES, the choice was made to reflect the measure-
ment data and the features available in these data.
The extracted dataset presented eight features with a one-hour time step throughout the
summer (01 December to 31 March) for N-simulated models. This data volume justified a
dimension reduction among the features, leading to the selection of the Principal Compo-
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
33
==PAGE:48==
nent Analysis projection. The first two principal components are utilized for the clustering
methods. The clustering method considered was k-means clustering described theoret-
ically in 2.6.2.4. This clustering method is based on DTW and has a predefined number
of clusters. Upon clustering, the results were visually displayed using a second PCA pro-
jection to depict the N TS as a single point in a two-dimensional space. This graphical
method assessed the qualitative performance of the clustering methods.
Finally, measurement data were projected with two PCA projections as described for the
simulation data. Likewise, only the summer period data were selected for the measure-
ment data.
In fact, a visual comparison between the measurement data and the simulation data is
essential to assess the feasibility of using the simulation input parameters to enhance
the measurement dataset with building and occupant characteristics from the simulation
dataset. Further clustering performance metrics are available in the machine learning
literature. However, further and more complex analysis went out of the scope of this
present work.
Concretely, both simulated and measured data were imported into Python. A filter was
applied to the dataset to keep the summer data. The data were scaled using standardiza-
tion, i.e. by removing the mean and unit of the variance and projected using PCA. This
data transformation resulted in a TS per simulated model outcome or household record.
Then, the simulated data were used for a TS k-means clustering using the tslearn library.
The complete code used for the TS clustering is described in Appendix A.4.3.
3.3.2
Features Sensitivity Analysis on Clustering results
Following the application of clustering methods, a two-fold sensitivity analysis was con-
ducted. The first aspect sought to confirm the optimal number of simulated BES models,
while the second focused on assessing the impact and implications of cluster labelling on
the system. The findings from the latter analysis provided vital insights into how cluster
labelling reflects building and occupant characteristics.
This section essentially serves as a precursor to using labels for the identification and allo-
cation of building and occupant characteristics to household records. Further, it facilitates
the training of machine learning algorithms on such enhanced measurement datasets.
However, the efficacy of this method will be thoroughly evaluated in Chapters 4 and 5.
The first phase of the sensitivity analysis leverages Mutual Information, a measure of the
similarity between two labels of the same data set [61]. The Mutual Information helps
measure the degree of influence each input parameter has on the cluster labels. Given
that a parametric simulation was selected for this study, two potential paths presented
themselves to determine the number of simulation needed: calculating all possible com-
binations or computing a representative sample that ensures the convergence of input
parameters. Due to computational efficiency considerations, we adopted the latter ap-
proach.
We determined convergence using Mutual Information. This was computed directly using
the scikit-learn package in Python and applied to different sub-samples (N=10 to N=400)
of the simulations. Once the Mutual Information of input parameters converged to a sta-
ble value, we knew that an adequate number of simulations had been completed. This
procedure assured the representativeness of the simulation sample.
The second phase of the sensitivity analysis employed the Pearson correlation coefficient.
Examining the Pearson coefficient of the cluster labels in relation to the others revealed
34
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:49==
the correlation, and hence the degree of dependence of the label on the various input
parameters. We utilized the Pearson coefficients to define the cluster with respect to the
different input parameters used for the simulation.
Mutual Information and Pearson correlation coefficient are both very similar coefficient.
However, Mutual Information is more general. It measures the information shared be-
tween two variables based on a large dataset whereas Pearson correlation coefficient
measures the linear relationship between two variables. This justifies the use of Mutual
Information to determine the number of simulation needed and the Pearson correlation
coefficient to describe the cluster labels given each input parameters.
3.3.3
Categorizing simulation inputs to simplify machine learning model
inputs
The simulation inputs play a vital role in the training of the end-goal machine learning
model. For the purpose of this study, the Random Forest Classifier was selected. The
model is trained with different levels of aggregated data from the raw input parameters
used to generate the Energy Plus models to a highly aggregated data set, which is a
more straightforward set of inputs. For example, a construction set for the wall is given
as different layers of different materials. It would be reduced to a U-value for the detailed
data set to just a categorical feature with heavy or light whether the wall contains concrete
or not.
Four aggregation levels were computed: Detailed, Reduced, Aggregated, and Highly -
Aggregated. They consist of various buildings, weather, and occupants characteristics
and are shown in Table 3.5. Note that Appendix A.1 provides a complete description of
each parameter. This way, we can manipulate the level of detail in our input data and,
as a result, the complexity of our models. The four aggregation levels are based on a
holistic approach based on the features importance from the Random Forest Classifier. It
comes from a reflection of the reduction of complexity that can be available in reality and
therefore facilitate the use by occupants.
Table 3.5: Parameters utilized in each feature set
Feature Set
Parameters
Highly-Aggregated
Weather, Type of House, Day Period, History Temperature
Aggregated
Weather, Building Geometry, Simple Building Construction (Con-
struction Type, Ceiling Insulation), Occupancy (Number of People),
Day Period, History Temperature
Reduced
Weather, Building Geometry, Simple Building Construction (Con-
struction Type, Ceiling Insulation), Building Envelope (Window-to-
Wall Ratio, Door Area), Occupancy (Number of People), Equipment
(P_equip, Elec_gas), HVAC, Day Period, History Temperature
Detailed
Weather, Building Geometry, Complex Building Construction (Wall
U-value, Roof U-value, Ground U-value, U-factor, SHGC), Building
