Number of People, Activity Level), Equipment (P_equip, Elec_gas),
HVAC, Lighting, Infiltration, Day Period, History Temperature
The target variable for the models is the Heat Stress Category, which can range from 0
(no risks) to 4 (extreme danger), described in Table 2.2 in Section 2.6.1.3.
For each set of features, a specific Random Forest Classifier model was trained. The
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
35
==PAGE:50==
Figure 3.7: Flowchart for the machine learning methodology
dataset was balanced to prevent class imbalance issues during training and split into
training and testing sets. The model’s performance was evaluated using accuracy as the
metric, and the duration of the training was also recorded. A confusion matrix was plotted
for each set of features, providing an illustrative summary of the model’s performance for
the four levels of aggregation.
Lastly, the feature importance was retrieved from the trained Random Forest Classifier
which computed the weight of the different features. This allowed us to understand which
features were most influential in predicting the heat stress category. This is particularly
useful when refining our models, as it can provide insight into which features we might
want to focus on or ignore.
Another technique than the aggregation method, based on automatic feature selection can
be used. The Random Forest Classifier provides the importance or weight of each feature
that can be used for selecting the most important feature. Using accuracy as a limitation
for the selection, the minimum required features are selected without compromising the
model performance. It uses cross-validation to evaluate the performance of the model.
See Appendix A.4.1, Listing A.10.
The results were used to conclude the influence of aggregation and simplifications of the
input and consequently, the simplification of the machine learning models.
3.4
Machine Learning Models Development
This last section discusses the machine learning algorithm selection and performance
evaluation. The classification algorithms were performed using a categorization of the
outcome, indoor air temperature and relative humidity aggregation in terms of apparent
temperature which is one of the methods for evaluating heat stress with a few input values
as discussed in Section 2.6.1 (See Section 3.4.1).
Afterwards, different machine learning models were evaluated with the selected aggrega-
tion level (See Section 3.4.2).
Finally, two algorithms were further investigated using a grid search cross-validation to
identify the best model’s parameters that resulted in the best performing model (See Sec-
tion 3.4.3).
3.4.1
Predicted Feature
The predicted feature chosen for this study is a heat stress category, which ranges from
0 (no discomfort, no heat stress) to 4 (extreme heat stress). The heat stress category is
based on the concept of AT computed using PyThermalComfort python package [62]. AT
was categorized as described in Section 2.6.1.3.
36
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:51==
3.4.2
Selection of Suitable Algorithms
A comparative analysis of multiple machine-learning models was performed to select the
most suitable algorithms for the classification task. Specifically, the LazyClassifier from
the lazypredict library was utilized in Python. This approach allowed for a quick evaluation
of different classifiers without fine-tuning models’ hyper-parameters. The analysis was
conducted on a dataset containing features related to indoor and outdoor conditions, as
described in Section 3.3.
First, the dataset was preprocessed and balanced to represent different heat stress cate-
gories equally. The method for balancing the data is down-sampling and using resample
from the sklearn library. Then, the dataset was split into training and testing sets using a
50
Next, the LazyClassifier was applied to the training and testing sets, providing an overview
of the performance of multiple classifiers. The models were evaluated using various met-
rics such as accuracy, precision, recall, and F1-score. The analysis results, including the
performance metrics for each model, were saved in a CSV file for further analysis and
comparison. Note that for computing time reasons, the LazyClassifier was executed for
14 days (01 January to 14 January) in the summer period.
The selection of appropriate algorithms was based on the performance of the classifiers
in terms of accuracy and other relevant metrics (Balanced Accuracy and F1-score). This
initial evaluation helped identify promising models for further investigation and parameter
tuning to optimize their performance. This latter is the object of the following Section. The
selection was made on both computing time and accuracy. The best model is the one
with high accuracy for a low computing time.
Note that the Python code used for the selection is given in Appendix A.4.1.
3.4.3
Assessment of Model Accuracy and Performance Metrics
A grid search cross-validation approach was employed using GridSearchCV from the
sklearn library to refine the selected models further and improve their performance. This
technique allows for systematically exploring different combinations of hyperparameters
to find the best configuration for the models.
Two models, namely RandomForestClassifier and BaggingClassifier, were chosen for
the grid search cross-validation. These models have demonstrated good performance
in previous studies and are suitable for classification tasks of predicting the heat stress
category.
The hyper-parameters were defined to explore different settings, such as the number
of estimators, maximum depth, minimum samples split, and minimum samples leaf as
stated in Tables 3.6 and 3.7. A 5-fold cross-validation was applied to ensure robust eval-
uation and leverage the accuracy variability influenced by the different sub-sampling of
the dataset into training and testing sub-datasets.

The grid search process involved fitting the models with the training data and evaluating
their performance using the defined scoring metrics. Based on the F1 score, the best
model was saved for future use. The grid search results, including the performance met-
rics for each hyper-parameter combination, were saved in CSV format.
By conducting the grid search cross-validation, it was possible to identify the optimal con-
figuration of the selected models, resulting in improved accuracy and performance for
the classification task. The best parameters and models obtained from this process were
saved for further analysis and evaluation. The results from the above sections are the
objects of Chapter 4.

Revisiting the objective of the simulation, Figure 4.2 illustrates the percentage of hours
each month within each heat stress category for the three climate scenarios.
For the present-day climate, the summer months (October to November) exhibit an ele-
vated risk, with over 50% of the time classified as Cautious to Danger, corresponding to
temperatures exceeding 27°C to 54°C.
For the projected 2050 and 2080 climates, the percentage of hours classified as Cautious
to Danger escalates significantly, with a diminution of the no-risk period to 10% in De-
cember and January and approximately 15% in February and November, compared to
around 25% in the present-day climate. The risk intensifies during the summer period but
also in the winter period, where the no-risk percentage decreases from 85% in June and
August to 75% in 2050 and 70% in 2080.
The juxtaposition of Figure 4.2 and Figure 4.1 indicates that the simulations tend to be
conservative. They exhibit a higher percentage in the Heat Stress categories of Extreme
Caution and Danger. For example, for Johannesburg, February displays an average of
40% caution across the samples and less than 20% in March. In contrast, the present-day
simulation demonstrates approximately 70% classified with a risk ranging from Caution to
Danger which is almost 2 times more time with heat-related risks. It should be noted that
the simulations were computed using a Johannesburg climate file on a highly simplified
model, and Figure 4.1 presents an average temperature across all the measured house-
holds. Nonetheless, Giyani measurements exhibit higher stress, also computed using an
average value across all the buildings. With percentages of around 15% in January and
February and 20% for November and December presenting no risk, Giyani data exhibits
similarity with simulated data. It should be noted that the Giyani climate was not utilized for
the simulation. This observation indicates that the risk is influenced by the typical weather
conditions, which may vary significantly from one year to another.
Figure 4.3 presents the average cumulative hours for a given apparent temperature across
the simulated models. This graph reveals a decline of 62% in the ”no risk” time over the
year when comparing the present-day climate to the projected climate for 2080 (from 54%
presenting no risk by today’s climate to only 33% by 2080). This reduction translates to
more than half of the year being subjected to at least a ”cautious” level of heat stress.
In the present-day climate, 7% of the year presents a ”danger” risk in heat stress. This
percentage increases to 13% for the projected climate of 2050 and further rises to 17%
for the 2080 climate projection.

Shacks as per the simulation. The figure reveals that indoor air temperatures are typically
higher than outdoor temperatures, with RDP houses experiencing higher temperatures

than Shacks. This discrepancy can be largely attributed to the higher mean air change

In conclusion, the analysis of simulated data and its comparison with measured data
demonstrates the reliability of using simulations to predict heat stress in buildings. The
simulations provide a comprehensive overview of the risks associated with standardized
climate scenarios and enable the assessment of building thermal behaviour under pro-
jected climates for 2050 and 2080. However, some discrepancies are noted when com-
pared with the measured data, leading to a more conservative approach in simulations,
which appears to present a more pessimistic outlook.
These simulated data could be leveraged to train the classification task. The primary
advantage of using simulation data is that the building and occupant characteristics are
known, as they were the input parameters for the simulation. Consequently, a more in-
depth comparison of the simulation and measurement data is suggested and will be the
focus of the next section. This comparison aims to assign input parameters to measure-
ment data if the similarity between the two datasets is proven. The similarity will be as-

4.3

4.3.1
Potential of data mining for data augmentation
The results for the TS clustering are presented in Figure 4.5. It illustrates the distribution
of the simulated samples among the two clusters computed with a k-means clustering
algorithm. The input parameters, described earlier 1, indicate that there are two types
of housing, RDP houses and Shacks, which are equally distributed (same quantity of
samples for both types). However, the results reveal a distribution of 46% in cluster 1
and 54% in cluster 2 for the successful simulations. This distribution approximates an
equal distribution. Therefore, exploring the relationship between the cluster labels and
the different input parameters, particularly the type of houses was intriguing. It should
be recalled that the objective of the clustering is to evaluate its potential to enhance our
measurement data set with building characteristics.
Figure 4.5: Cluster distribution
Figure 4.6 displays the distribution of input parameters given the cluster labels. Categori-
cal parameters are depicted using histograms that count the occurrences of each category
for each parameter in every cluster. Continuous parameters are depicted using box plots
showing the variance around a mean value for the clusters. A qualitative analysis of the
results reveals a clear identification of the role of specific parameters in the clustering.
The house type shows more RDP houses in cluster 1, whereas cluster 2 better describes
the Shacks. Parameters inherent to the type of house, i.e., u-values for walls, roof, and
floor, floor area, volume, number of people, ceiling insulation, construction type, and infil-
tration rate, exhibit an unequal distribution among the two clusters, whereas parameters
independent from house types exhibit equal distribution, i.e., orientation, window u-value,
activity level, number of lights, door area, equipment power. In conclusion, there is a
correlation between cluster labelling and building and occupant characteristics.
The PCA two-dimensional projection with the simulated data, as shown in Figure 4.7,
demonstrated a clear differentiation of the clusters among the samples. The dimension
reduction of each TS sample is represented by a point, and cluster 1 and cluster 2 are
distinctly differentiated in the two-dimensional space. It appears that the values with a

clusters. It does not correspond to the group division shown in Table 2.1, which refers
to 8 informal housing (Shacks), 13 apartments, 10 formal housing, 13 pre-1994 low-cost

for Cluster 5. It somewhat resembles the 13 apartments, 13 pre-1994 low-cost housing.
However, the distribution is not entirely similar. Moreover, the paper does not include all
the samples present in the data set, which make it harder to reverse-engineer the paper
and therefore assign cluster labels which are meaningful for the dataset. Uncertainties on

the actual behaviour of the samples and their attributions to one type of known building

In conclusion, the PCA approach demonstrates the potential of using clustering methods
such as K-means clustering to compare measurement and simulation data, thereby ad-

clusters in the measurement data is not clear, as seen in the Johannesburg sample. Fur-
thermore, there is no clear overlap observed between the measurement and simulation
data in the PCA two-dimensional projection.
These results suggest that using measurement data for classification tasks may be overly
optimistic, as the assignment of building and occupant characteristics may not be mean-
ingful. It’s important to remember that in the context of a longer study period, more on-site
measurements can be performed in Africa, and this approach may not be necessary if the
basic building and occupant characteristics are known.
4.3.2
Features Sensitivity Analysis on clustering results
The sensitivity analysis indicates the relationship between the cluster labels and the differ-
ent input parameters. This analysis is first performed to evaluate the number of samples
to simulate. Figure 4.9 provides the mutual information for each parameter calculated with
a defined number of simulations. It shows that no total convergence among all the pa-
rameters is achieved with more simulations. However, upon closer inspection, it is clear
that the envelope composition (u-values) and building geometry (floor area) have a more
significant influence than values such as activity level, heating coil power, window-to-wall
ratio, or number of light bulbs. Overall, parameters representing building characteristics,
internal gains and occupant characteristics tend to converge in the same zone. Therefore,
Figure 4.9 suggests that approximately 400 simulations seem to be an adequate number
of simulations. In fact, it shows that these input parameters mentioned earlier remain in
the same order of mutual information above 300 simulations. As the developed script
failed between 12 and 16% of the models due to geometric reasons, 500 models were
simulated. Note that the mutual information values are available in Appendix A.3.2.
46
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:61==
Figure 4.9: Mutual Information computed for different numbers of samples randomly
drawn out of all the simulation data
Secondly, the sensitivity analysis indicates the influence of each input parameter on the
cluster label.
The sensitivity analysis performed with the Pearson Coefficient matrix,
shown in Figure 4.10, shows the influences between each input parameter. However, the
task was to characterize the cluster label. Consequently, only the cluster label (cluster),
given all the input parameters, is the object of the present work’s interest. This shows that
the floor area and volume of the building, as well as construction type (heavy or lightweight
construction), the insulation of the ceiling, and envelope composition somewhat correlate
with the cluster labels. These aforementioned parameters present a Pearson coefficient
over 0.3 in absolute terms. In contrast, building orientation, window-to-wall ratio, door
area, activity level, equipment power, heating coil power, light bulbs, and infiltration rate
have less influence on the cluster label. Note that the number of occupants (number of
people) has the same influence as building envelope characteristics in absolute terms.
In conclusion, the sensitivity analysis and the clustering method collectively suggest that
the number of parametric simulations should be at least in the order of 500 to yield stable
results. Interestingly, the cluster labels seem to be associated with building characteristics
and certain occupant behaviors, such as the number of occupants. As a result, these
labels could be utilized to aggregate building information into a proxy (cluster label) for
machine learning applications.
The cluster labels define the building characteristics, including basic geometry, envelope
indicators, and the number of occupants in the building. They also highlight the most
significant features that could be used in the classification task. The next section will focus
on a more detailed approach for determining the aggregation of features and simplifying
input parameters into parameters that occupants can easily encode themselves.

4.3.3
Categorizing simulation inputs to simplify machine learning model
inputs
As delineated in Section 3.3.3, the input parameters were manipulated to reduce their
complexity. Figure 4.11 exhibits the accuracy of the models executed for the four lev-
els of aggregation, as described in the four feature sets, ranging from the detailed input
parameters to the highly-aggregated input parameters set. It arises that the aggregated
input parameters set yields results comparable to those of the detailed input parame-
ters. Furthermore, the model training time predictably diminishes with a decrease in the
complexity level of the input parameters. The aggregated feature set offers an optimal
balance, providing high accuracy with reduced model training time.
Figure 4.11: Random Forest Classifier accuracies and model training duration for each
feature set
Figures 4.12 present the confusion matrices for each feature set aggregation case. These
matrices indicate that the three aggregation sets (detailed, reduced, aggregated) provide
accurate predictions of the heat stress categories (higher density of values on the diagonal
line). However, these confusion matrices reveal that the model tends to be conservative
in its predictions. Some heat stress categories that are 3 are classified as 4, but fewer
samples that are 4 are predicted as lower heat stress categories. This observation holds
for the first three feature sets. Conversely, the highly-aggregated feature set poorly pre-
dicts heat stress categories in a non-conservative manner. Some samples classified in
4 are predominantly predicted in 3 and 2. This last feature set is unsuitable for further
experiments. Consequently, the aggregated feature set emerges as the most promising.

The feature importance computation for the 4 described feature sets is available in Ap-

As an extension of this work, a feature selection method was employed to automatically
select the features of higher importance while performing a Random Forest Classifier.
This resulted in the determination that outdoor dry bulb temperature, atmospheric pres-
sure, floor area, volume, orientation, ceiling insulation, number of people, period of the
day, and history temperature are the minimum required parameters for training the model
with an average accuracy of 81% in 5-folds cross-validation. However, the presence of
atmospheric pressure appears surprising. According to this final experiment, the enve-
lope composition is not necessary to achieve satisfactory performance. Therefore, the
building construction type can be omitted as long as the presence of insulation in the ceil-
ing is known, as well as the building geometry and the number of occupants alongside
the outdoor conditions.
In conclusion, the input parameters used for defining the BES models can be effectively
simplified into an aggregated feature set without undermining the model’s performance,
as evaluated by its accuracy. The aggregated feature set, chosen for the classification
task, presents an optimal balance between simplicity and performance.
50
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:65==
It’s important to note that these features were aggregated using a comprehensive method.
An automated selection process was carried out, yielding results similar to the suggested
aggregation, thereby validating the relevance of this aggregated feature set.
However, the automated feature set selection was somewhat risky, as it resulted in the
categorization of various input parameters into simple categories. These categories are
presumed to be more comprehensible to non-experts, especially with proper explanation
(for instance, in an app format). An automatic selection from all available features, both
aggregated and non-aggregated, could lead to the selection of wall composition encoded
in U-value, while the aggregated form provides a general assessment of the construction
(heavy or lightweight construction).
Therefore, the automatic selection served more as a validation tool rather than the central
part of the analysis.
Consequently, the dataset is formed with the aggregated feature set to perform the clas-
sification task which is the focus of the next section.

Machine Learning models development
This section focuses on the prediction of indoor heat stress using machine learning clas-

4.4.1
Selection of appropriate algorithms
As illustrated in Figure 4.13, a comparative analysis of 25 different classifiers, based on
accuracy and model training duration, was conducted to identify the most effective clas-
sifiers. As depicted in the figure, accuracies range from 85% to 25%, contingent on the
algorithm employed. These accuracies may fluctuate depending on the hyper-parameters
utilized in the model. Nevertheless, the hyper-parameters are automatically configured
within the used Python library. This facilitates the selection of the most effective model,
which can then be further fine-tuned. Consequently, the results indicate that the Bagging
Classifier, Decision Tree Classifier, and Random Forest Classifier are the three most ef-
fective classifiers, and they exhibit relatively low computation time for training, which aligns
with our objectives. Subsequent analysis will focus on the Random Forest Classifier and
the Bagging Classifier to test the previously used model and the best-performing model.

surpasses the Random Forest Classifier in performance, providing a high-performance
machine learning classification model for subsequent research.

In conclusion, even in the face of limited measurement data, the use of simulated data has
proven to be a powerful tool in performing the classification task. The employed workflow
underscores the potential of clustering methods, such as utilizing cluster labels as surro-
gates for building and occupant characteristics. Techniques such as PCA analysis and
clustering offer considerable promise for drawing comparisons between known and un-
known buildings based on their data records. This approach addresses the issue of data
scarcity, facilitating predictions even in situations where complete information isn’t readily
available. The utility of cluster label proxies extends to encapsulating basic geometry,
envelope characteristics, and the number of building occupants.
Simulated data were produced using complex input parameters that might not be readily
understandable to non-experts. Consequently, various levels of data aggregation were
explored, culminating in an optimal level of aggregation. This level encompasses cate-
gorical variables of building parameters and the number of occupants, along with weather
data that could be sourced through a weather service API.
Lastly, the Bagging and Random Forest models, both instances of ensemble machine
learning models, demonstrated superior performance in predicting indoor heat stress.
These models achieved impressive accuracy rates of 91% and 87% respectively.

5
Discussion and Future Work
This chapter aims to synthesize the results and evaluate the potential of employing ma-
chine learning techniques to predict heat stress, thereby improving indoor thermal comfort
in low-income housing in Sub-Saharan Africa, specifically in Southern Africa. The discus-
sion will address this study’s results, limitations, and assumptions.
5.1
Interpretation and Significance of the Results
The scarcity of data provided by the SAMRC due to ethical considerations necessitated
exploring data mining techniques in this study, explicitly using k-means clustering on time
series data. The objective was to augment the dataset and potentially utilize the avail-
able measured data to train a classification model for heat stress prediction. This method
demonstrated promising results, revealing evident correlations between the cluster labels
produced by the clustering techniques and the building characteristics and occupant be-
haviour.
Nevertheless, a discernible gap persists between the measured and simulated data, likely
attributable to the extreme simplification of the simulated model. The initial objective was
to represent as comprehensively as possible the various combinations of building charac-
teristics present in the buildings assessed by [9] and [18]. However, numerous assump-
tions were made during the process due to the lack of detailed descriptions of the input
parameters in the referenced articles.
As a result, the findings do not conclusively support using clustering from simulated data to
assign building characteristics. It is important to note that this method was explored within
the constraints of this thesis, influenced by the available time and resources. Given the
impossibility of collecting more precise data for this context, the focus was on extracting
as much information as possible from the available given data.
In this study, the primary focus was on applying K-means clustering, a method that carries
inherent limitations related to the initialization process and the selection of K. The value
of K is often determined either by a pre-defined number of clusters or by assessing the
sum-of-square errors function with varying K values. When the number of clusters is
unknown, it is recommended to select a K value by examining the convergence of the sum-
of-square error function. However, for the scope of this master’s thesis, K was defined as
the expected number of clusters (5 for the measurements from Johannesburg and 2 for
the simulated data).
The initiation of the clustering process in this study employed the default method used by
the tslearn library, which involves the selection of random centroids for the initial cluster
computation. As a result, the convergence of the K-means clustering, which is predicated
on the minimization of the error function, may settle on a local minimum rather than an
absolute minimum. This could potentially lead to a suboptimal attribution of cluster labels.
Therefore, exploring further K-means clustering using alternative initialization techniques
would be advantageous. For instance, multiple initialization starting points could be em-
ployed, which would involve selecting different random locations for the initial centroids.
Consequently, the model that minimizes the error function at the lower level could be cho-
sen among various trained models and would likely correspond to an absolute minimum.
54
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:69==
Nevertheless, the approach done in this master’s thesis was sufficient to give insights and
reliable results under the scope of this work.
In recent years, there has been an increased interest in energy demand forecasting
and energy efficiency of large building stocks, often employing energy signature or other
simulation-based techniques. Clustering methods are frequently used in these contexts to
compare building heating energy consumption with other buildings, often for the purpose
of fault detection. Therefore, it is promising to use k-means clustering for the purpose of
data augmentation on buildings when data are unavailable as the present work suggested.
For future research, it would be beneficial to collect more precise data or further investigate
clustering methods with measured data and higher-level simulations, which were beyond
the scope of this master’s thesis.
5.2
Evaluation of the Early Alerting System for Heat Stress
Assessment
The scarcity of data necessitates further research to evaluate the EAS for heat stress.
In this study, only simulated data were available for model training. A comparison of the
measured data with the simulation outputs, particularly the records from the Giyani house-
holds, reveals similarities in terms of the distribution of heat stress categories. These cat-
egories were determined based on the samples’ monthly mean indoor temperature and
relative humidity.
However, a noticeable discrepancy was observed between the measured data from Jo-
hannesburg and the simulation records, which were generated using Johannesburg weather
files. This offset can be attributed to the annual variations in actual weather conditions.
Despite this, the simulations and Johannesburg’s measured data encompass the same
range of values for heat stress categories.
Therefore, using a combination of measured and simulated data for model training is
recommended, followed by assessing the model’s performance using measured data.
This combination of datasets, including a simulated dataset alongside measurements,
could enhance the model’s ability to predict extreme temperatures and exceptional heat
waves accurately. It could also facilitate adaptation to projected climate scenarios, as
suggested during the master’s thesis using IPCC predictions for 2050 and 2080.
Regarding this IPCC predictions, exploring the new projection models based on RCPs
would be beneficial. These pathways provide a comprehensive framework for under-
standing future climate scenarios, which could enhance the effectiveness of the EAS for
heat stress assessment. They leverage the hypothesis made in the model used in this
work which was based on an older version of IPCC work; however, the latter is largely
democratised with various tools online, i.e. CCWorldWeatherGen.
5.3
Comparative Analysis of Various Machine Learning
Models
The selection of classification machine learning models in this study was facilitated by
LazyPredict, a tool that obviates the need for hyper-parameters used for fine-tuning the
models. While an ideal scenario would involve a more comprehensive estimation of ma-
chine learning model performance through a grid search cross-validation fine-tuning pro-
cess, the methodology employed in this study aligns with the primary aim of this mas-
ter’s thesis. This aim was to explore potential methodologies and frameworks rather than
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
55
==PAGE:70==
solely focusing on achieving superior accuracies. As such, the simplifications made do
not significantly impact the final results.
Furthermore, it is worth noting that previous studies, such as the one conducted by [36],
have identified Random Forest and Decision Tree algorithms as outperforming other clas-
sifiers. This finding aligns with the results of the present study, further validating the ef-
fectiveness of these models in the context of heat stress prediction. Thus, despite the
absence of hyper-parameter fine-tuning, the selected machine-learning models demon-
strate robust performance, underscoring the potential of machine learning in advancing
indoor thermal comfort research.
In contrast to other related studies, this master’s thesis considers class imbalance when
predicting heat stress, which improves accuracy across the spectrum. However, using the
downsampling method introduces certain limitations. In fact, downsampling was chosen
instead of oversampling for computational time purposes. Addressing class imbalance is
highly recommended in this study due to the over-representation of lower risks of heat
stress. If the class imbalance issue is not treated, classifying all instances in the most
represented heat stress category can immediately improve the model’s accuracy while
potentially underperforming in predicting extreme temperature ranges, which would un-
dermine our effort to predict heat stress problems accurately. However, downsampling
can result in a loss of information. Therefore, it is necessary to apply this method to larger
datasets and assess the influence of downsampling on the results in greater detail. This
remains an area for future exploration.
5.4
Implications of the Results
The results from the BES exhibit a similar categorization of heat stress over time, consis-
tent with the studies conducted in [9] using our sample from Giyani, a rural setting. Ad-
ditionally, these findings demonstrate that when assessing the potential impact of future
climate scenarios, there is a congruent consensus predicting an increase in heat stress in
low-income housing. The associated risk appears to escalate annually, as substantiated
by [9]. Studies [18] and [20] also suggest that indoor temperatures tend to exceed outdoor
temperatures.
Clustering methodologies revealed a correlation between cluster labels and a combination
of building and occupant characteristics. This finding is congruent with those of [46], which
found correlations between cluster designations and the socio-economic background of
inhabitants.
Roof insulation emerged as a particularly significant factor in our models predicting heat
stress. This implies that the presence or absence of roof insulation significantly impacts
the level of heat stress experienced within the household, corroborating results found in
[18] and [9].
Concerning machine learning, the level of accuracy achieved aligns with that of [65],
which reported a Decision Tree classifier attaining a 92% accuracy (F1-score) on test
data. Lower performance was observed when training with a new data set. In contrast,
our current study employed a split in training and testing data, and the average accuracy
achieved through 5-fold cross-validation suggests an accuracy rate of 90% for the Bag-
ging classifier and 87% for the Random Forest on a class-balanced data set. Further
analyses would aim to test the model on measured data.
56
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:71==
5.5
Discussion of Limitations and Potential Improvements
In conclusion, this study’s limitations and potential improvements are primarily driven by
the scarcity of data and the attempt to augment this data with simulated information. To
enhance the outcomes of this research, it is strongly recommended to increase the com-
plexity of the simulations by using more accurate building dimensions and reducing as-
sumptions through in-situ parameter measurements. This approach would better repre-
sent reality and provide more accurate simulated models. This remains an area for future
development.
Furthermore, improving the quality of measured data and knowledge of corresponding
internal gains and building characteristics would significantly enhance the potential of us-
ing such data as input for BES models. These models could increase the representativity
of other simulation results, i.e. mean radiant temperature, and globe temperature, which
could be used to calculate better heat stress indicators as WBGT. It would undoubtedly
result in better heat stress assessment and, in combination with classification machine
learning models, would result in better prediction.
Integrating simulated and measured data could improve the accuracy of extreme tem-
perature predictions and make the model more adaptable to changing climates. This is
because it could consider predicted climate scenarios using state-of-the-art climate pre-
diction models based on RCP. This approach would address the current study’s limita-
tions and pave the way for more robust and accurate heat stress prediction models in the
future.
Other factors such as indoor air quality, are defined as being also critical in low-income
housing, correlation between bad indoor air quality combined with extreme heat events
needs to be further studied as this work overlooked that part. It remains crucial to assess
indoor environmental quality (air quality and thermal comfort). [21]
Lastly, the application of forecasting machine learning models, particularly those based on
time series data, is swiftly gaining prominence in the realm of energy demand forecasting
for large building stocks. Such forecasting models were not explored in the current study,
which implicitly relied on weather forecasts to predict heat stress categories. These fore-
casting models present a promising avenue for eliminating the dependency on weather
forecasts, potentially utilizing indoor temperature and relative humidity monitoring within
buildings instead. However, implementing such models necessitates using advanced
technology, which is currently not readily available in the context of low-income housing
in Southern Africa.
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
57
==PAGE:72==
6
Conclusion
The increasing frequency of extreme heat events due to global warming and climate
change is a global concern.
However, the impact is unevenly distributed, with Sub-
Saharan African dwellings often overlooked despite the direct impact of heat stress on
the health and socio-economic development of their inhabitants. The IPCC has under-
scored the urgency of assessing and implementing prevention methods to cope with heat
stress. EAS, which relies heavily on machine learning models, is a crucial tool that can
aid authorities in preventing and communicating about extreme heat exposure. These
models offer a less complex alternative to BES.
In this context, the present work focused on using artificial intelligence to predict indoor
temperature and assess heat stress in Sub-Saharan African buildings subjected to climate
change.
6.1
Can Building Energy Simulation and on-site
measurements be associated with developing an
accurate machine learning model and leveraging sparse
data issues?
A machine learning model has been designed to predict heat stress categories within
simulated data, reflecting the conditions typical of low-income urban dwellings in Johan-
nesburg, South Africa. The use of simulated data has been vital in enhancing and refining
the existing measured dataset, which was initially found lacking in essential information re-
quired for efficient machine learning applications. The potential to create a hybrid dataset
that merges measured and simulated data is evaluated. This approach can be especially
useful in extracting building and occupant characteristics to supplement missing informa-
tion in the measured dataset. Enhanced on-site measurements and a detailed compari-
son of clustering techniques, supported by an in-depth understanding of the context, could
be adequate to meet this challenge.
In this regard, the application of clustering methods has shown that cluster labels carry in-
formation about the characteristics of buildings and occupants, serving as a proxy. These
labels are tied to simple geometry parameters of the building and the number of occupants
proven by high Pearson correlation coefficients between labels and these parameters.
They also show significant correlations with the type of housing (RDP houses or shacks).
However, they were not linked to the house orientation or activity level but showed a
strong dependency on envelope characteristics like ceiling insulation.
When clustering was applied only to Johannesburg’s measurements, it was found that the
cluster distribution bore a close resemblance to the distributions of houses mentioned in
the article, but was not an exact match. This discrepancy prevented the exclusive use of k-
means clustering on measurements to approximate building characteristics, necessitating
a comparative analysis with the simulation using PCA.
The PCA has revealed the potential of using k-means clustering to assign characteris-
tics of buildings and occupants to measurement records that are missing parts of the
data. The combination of on-site measurements and more realistic simulation models
could potentially address current challenges (such as the lack of clear overlap between
measurements and simulation data).
58
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:73==
In the context of heat stress analysis, simulations and measurements have demonstrated
similarities, with about 20% of cases showing no risks due to heat in January under the
present-day climate and Giyani data. Nevertheless, the data from Johannesburg’s mea-
surements seemed to be more optimistic compared to the simulations, potentially due to
variations between years, despite being computed with Johannesburg’s climate. The sim-
ulation data also indicated that indoor temperatures were generally higher than outdoor
ones, thereby increasing the potential for heat-related illnesses indoors. However, these
temperatures showed a marked improvement with an increased air change rate with lower
indoor temperature in Shacks than RDP houses which present a higher air change rate.
In conclusion, the collaborative use of BES and on-site measurements exhibits significant
potential for creating an augmented dataset for the classification task. Considering that
simulations can be calculated based on projected climate scenarios, the incorporation
of BES may enhance the predictive capabilities of the model, particularly in forecasting
increasingly frequent extreme temperature events that are likely to occur in the future.
6.2
How to reduce the complexity of building and occupant
characteristics to be easily interpreted by occupants
themselves?
The complexity of input parameters for the models was significantly streamlined to a few
readily comprehensible factors without sacrificing the precision of the model. These es-
sential parameters encompass weather conditions (accessible from weather services),
floor area, building height, construction type (lightweight or heavyweight), the existence
of roof insulation, and the number of occupants. This aggregation led to a decrease in
the training duration of the Random Forest Classifier while preserving its performance in-
tegrity. The model yielded an accuracy of 87% before fine-tuning, which was marginally
lower than the model trained with more detailed input parameters. Analysis of the confu-
sion matrix at each feature detail level indicated that this simplification of complexity did
not adversely affect performance. However, the high level of aggregation using only the
type of house as a feature did not perform well, indicating the boundaries of such ag-
gregation. This aggregation design allows for easier interaction by non-experts, enabling
them to encode variables using simple descriptions without requiring detailed knowledge
of factors like U-values or the composition of the building envelope.
6.3
How accurately can the machine learning models predict
indoor heat stress?
The Random Forest Classifier and Bagging Classifier showcased high F1-scores, achiev-
ing 87% and 91% respectively, when subjected to 5-fold cross-validation on simulation
data. These machine learning models incorporated features about weather conditions -
including Outdoor Dry Bulb Temperature, Relative Humidity, Wind Speed, Wind Direction,
Precipitable Water, and Atmospheric Pressure - and construction-related factors like the
type of construction (lightweight or heavyweight, depending on the presence of concrete
in the walls), number of occupants, presence of ceiling insulation, period of the day (used
as a proxy for solar radiation), and temperature history over the preceding 6 hours.
Out of 25 different classification models evaluated, the selected models, Bagging and
Random Forest outperformed the rest in terms of training time and accuracy. This out-
come reinforces the superior performance of ensemble models over simpler classification
models, a sentiment echoed by other related studies. The simplicity of input parameters
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
59
==PAGE:74==
and reduced training time makes these models particularly suited for convenient deploy-
ment through APIs and mobile applications.
6.4
How does climate change influence indoor heat stress?
Simulations for low-income housing suggest that the likelihood of indoor heat stress may
escalate in the future. These projections show a significant 62% reduction in the no-risk
category, which corresponds to an apparent temperature below 27°C. By 2080, this would
result in only 33% of the time during the year falling into the no-risk category, compared
to 54% in the current climate conditions.
While instances of heat stress exceeding 54°C are limited on average, projections for 2080
suggest that 17% of the time will fall into the ’Danger’ category (apparent temperatures
between 40°C and 54°C). This is an increase from 13% projected for 2050 and 7% in
the present-day climate scenario. These increases are significant in terms of the annual
duration of potential heat stress.
Given that heat stress peaks during the summer, the absence of cooler periods during
the night could lead to an increase in heat-related illnesses. Furthermore, heat stress is
projected to rise even during the winter season, leading to ’Extreme Caution’ events.
These projections, based on future climate scenarios, emphasize the necessity for EAS
and public interventions. The incorporation of passive solutions, such as shading and
ceiling insulation, could help mitigate the impact of extreme indoor apparent temperatures.
Therefore, it is imperative to integrate projected climate files into machine learning models,
to develop models capable of adapting to future climate scenarios.

Building Energy Simulation Model definitions

Parameters (1/2)
Parameters
Description
Floor Area (m2)
The floor area in square meters.
Building Height (m)
height in meters.
Building Length (m)
The length in meters.
Building Width (m)
The width in meters.
Window-to-Wall Ratio
(-)
The ratio of window area to wall area.
Number of People (-)
The number of people living.
Activity Level (W)
The activity level of the people in watts.
P_equip (W)
The power consumption of equipment in watts.
P_hvac (W)
The power consumption of the HVAC system in watts.
Wall Construction List
The list of materials used for the wall construction.
Roof Construction List
The list of materials used for the roof construction.
Floor
Construction
List
The list of materials used for the floor construction.
Ground Construction
List
The list of materials used for the ground construction.
Door Height (m)
The height of the door in meters.
Door Width (m)
The width of the door in meters.
Infiltration Rate (m3/s)
The rate at which outside air infiltrates into the shack in cubic meters
per second.
U-Factor (W/(m2K))
The U-factor of the windows, which measures the rate of heat trans-
fer through the windows.
SHGC (-)
The solar heat gain coefficient of the windows, indicating the
amount of solar radiation that enters the shack through the win-
dows.
Heating Setpoint (° C)
The desired temperature setpoint for heating.
Orientation (°)
The orientation of the shack in degrees from North.
Type of House
The type of house, specifically a shack.

of
Lighting
Bulbs (-)
The number of light bulbs.
Electric or Gas (Elec/-
Gas)
The type of equipment system, either electric or gas-powered.

Table A.2: Input Parameters, Indoor Conditions, Weather Parameters, and Computed
Parameters (2/2)
Parameters
Description
Indoor Mean Air Tem-
perature (° C)
The average air temperature inside the shack.
Indoor
Air
Relative
Humidity (%)
The relative humidity inside the shack.
Outdoor
Dry
Bulb
Temperature (° C)
The outdoor air temperature as measured by a dry bulb thermome-
ter.
Outdoor Relative Hu-
midity (%)
The relative humidity of the outdoor air.
Atmospheric
Station
Pressure (Pa)
The atmospheric pressure at the location of the shack.
Wind Direction
The direction from which the wind is blowing.
Wind Speed (m/s)
The speed of the wind.
Precipitable Water
The total amount of water vapour present in a column of the atmo-
sphere if all the water vapour were to condense and fall as rain.
Apparent
Tempera-
ture (° C)
Computed from the indoor mean air temperature and indoor air rel-
ative humidity.
History
Temperature
(° C)
The rolling mean of the outdoor dry bulb temperature over the past
6 time units.
Day Period
Derived from the hour of the timestamp. Defined as ’morning’ (6-
12), ’afternoon’ (12-18), ’evening’ (18-22), or ’night’ (22-6).
Heat Index (° C)
Computed from the outdoor dry bulb temperature and outdoor rel-
ative humidity.
Heat Stress Category
Categorizes the apparent temperature into 5 levels: 0 (< 27° C), 1
(27-32° C), 2 (32-38° C), 3 (38-41° C), and 4 (≥41° C).

publicly available at https://github.com/bruadam/ICIEE_DTU_Heat_stress_Africa.
Contact Caradee Wright SAMRC for the measurements data at caradee.wrigth@mrc.ac.za
and Joe Matsapola at Joe.Matsapola@weathersa.co.za for the historical weather data.

# This script is used to run the simulation and generate the results for a
given set of parameters and N sets of inputs for the simulation. N is the
number of parametric runs.
2
3
# Last updated: 2023-06-24
4
5
#%% LIBRARIES
6
import pandas as pd
7
import numpy as np
8
import sys
9
import time
10
from IPython.display import clear_output
11
12
from pyosmodel import utils
13
from pyosmodel.model import simulate_model
14
from pyosmodel.predefinedmodel import define_RDP_inputs , define_shack_inputs
15
from pyosmodel.params import change_timestep
16
from pyosmodel.simulation import run_simulation
17
18
#%% SETTINGS
19
# Number of simulations to run
20
Number_simulations = 500 # Must be even
21
N = int(Number_simulations/2)
22
23
# Definition of the inputs for the simulation
24
inputs = pd.DataFrame()
25
inputs = define_RDP_inputs(N)
26
inputs = pd.concat([inputs, define_shack_inputs(N)],
27
ignore_index=True)
28
29
# Definition of required directories
30
eplus_install_dir=r'C:/EnergyPlusV23 -1-0'
31
32
# Setting up the output parameters
33
output_data = ['Zone Air Relative Humidity',
34
'Zone Mean Air Temperature',
35
'Zone Mean Radiant Temperature',
36
'People Occupant Count',
37
'Lights Total Heating Rate',
38
'Electric Equipment Total Heating Rate',
39
'Zone Windows Total Heat Loss Rate',
40
'Zone Windows Total Heat Gain Rate',
41
'Infiltration Air Change Rate',
42
'Zone Infiltration Air Change Rate',
43
'Zone Thermostat Heating Setpoint Temperature']
44
45
# Setting up the simulation timestep
46
step = 4 # one value every 15 minutes
47
48
output_directory_paths = ['simulations/present -day/',
49
'simulations/2050/',
50
'simulations/2080/']
51
52
epw_paths = [r'database/climate/ZAF_GT_Johannesburg.Botanical.Gardens.683610
_TMYx.2004-2018.epw',
53
r'database/climate/ZAF_GT_Johannesburg.Botanical.Gardens.683610
_TMYx.2004-2018_2050.epw',
54
r'database/climate/ZAF_GT_Johannesburg.Botanical.Gardens.683610
_TMYx.2004-2018_2080.epw']

55
56
for output_directory_path , epw_path in zip(output_directory_paths , epw_paths):
57
# Save the inputs in a csv file
58
inputs.to_csv(output_directory_path + 'inputs.csv')
59
# Setting up the output directory
60
utils.output_directory(output_directory_path)
61

# Storing the failed simulations inputs in a csv file for exploration
63
failed_simulations = pd.DataFrame()
64
65
#%% SIMULATION
66
print('Starting simulation')
67
start = time.time()
68
69
# Simulation Core
70
for i in range(Number_simulations):
71
try:
72
# Define the model
73
item_inputs = inputs.loc(axis=0)[i]
74
model = simulate_model(item_inputs , output_data , epw_path)
75
change_timestep(model, step)
76
77
# Save the model in an idf file
78
output_directory = output_directory_path + 'outputs/' + str(i) + '
/'
79
idf_path = output_directory_path + 'models/' + str(i) + '.idf'
80
utils.save_model_to_idf(model, idf_path)
81
82
# Run the simulation
83
result = run_simulation(idf_path , epw_path , output_directory ,
eplus_install_dir)
84
85
except:
86
# Concatenate the failed simulations
87
failed_simulations = pd.concat([failed_simulations , item_inputs],
axis=1)
88
continue
89
utils.clear_screen()
90
# For every 10 simulations , print the progress
91
if i % 10 == 0:
92
duration = time.time() - start
93
estimated_time_left = duration/(i+1)*(Number_simulations -i-1)/60
94
#
95
print('Progression ' + str(i) + '/' + str(Number_simulations) + '
Estimated time left:' + str(estimated_time_left) + '\n\n\n')
96
97
utils.clear_screen()
98
# Save the failed simulations in a csv file
99
failed_simulations.to_csv(output_directory_path + 'failed_simulations.csv'
)
100
101
end = time.time()
102
print('Total time: ' + str(end - start))
103
print('{} Simulations failed'.format(failed_simulations.shape[1]))
104
print('Finished one set of simulations')
105
106
print('Finished')

import numpy as np
9
import pandas as pd
10
import os
11
from toolbox.utils import import_saws_data
12
import warnings
13
warnings.filterwarnings('ignore')
14
15
16
path = '../database/johannesburg/weather/'
17
files = os.listdir(path)
18
19
temp = pd.DataFrame()
20
hum = pd.DataFrame()
21
pres = pd.DataFrame()
22
windspeed = pd.DataFrame()
23
rain = pd.DataFrame()
24
winddir = pd.DataFrame()
25
26
for file in files:
27
if file.endswith('_temp.csv'):
28
temp = pd.concat([temp, import_saws_data(path + file)])
29
elif file.endswith('_humidity.csv'):
30
hum = pd.concat([hum, import_saws_data(path + file)])
31
elif file.endswith('_pressure.csv'):
32
pres = pd.concat([pres, import_saws_data(path + file)])
33
elif file.endswith('_windspeed.csv'):
34
windspeed = pd.concat([windspeed , import_saws_data(path + file)])
35
elif file.endswith('_rain.csv'):
36
rain = pd.concat([rain, import_saws_data(path + file)])
37
elif file.endswith('_winddir.csv'):
38
winddir = pd.concat([winddir , import_saws_data(path + file)])
39
elif file.endswith('.csv'):
40
print('{} is not a valid file'.format(file))
41
42
# Concatenate all dataframes
43
df = pd.concat([temp, hum, pres, rain, winddir , windspeed], axis=1)
44
df.columns = ['Outdoor Dry Bulb Temperature', 'Outdoor Relative Humidity', '
Atmospheric Station Pressure', 'Precipitable Water', 'Wind Direction', '
Wind Speed']

', 0)
53
df['Precipitable Water'] = df['Precipitable Water'].astype(float)
54
55
df['Wind Speed'] = df['Wind Speed'].fillna(0)
56
57
df['Atmospheric Station Pressure'] = df['Atmospheric Station Pressure'].fillna
(method='ffill')
58
59
df['Wind Speed'] = df['Wind Speed'].replace('
-', np.nan)

# Defintion of a material list from a imported material database
4
'''
5
Description: This function creates a list of materials from a given material
database. The material database is a list of lists, where each list
contains the following information about a material: name, thickness ,
conductivity , density , specific heat, thermal absorptance and solar
absorptance. The function returns a list of materials , which can be used
to create a construction.
6
Input: model, material_database
7
Output: material_list
8
'''
9
def create_material_list(model, material_database):
10
material_list = []
11
for material in material_database:
12
material_name = material[0]
13
material_thickness = material[1]
14
material_conductivity = material[2]
15
material_density = material[3]
16
material_specific_heat = material[4]
17
material_thermal_absorptance = material[5]
18
material_solar_absorptance = material[6]
19
20
material = op.model.StandardOpaqueMaterial(model)
21
material.setName(material_name)
22
material.setThickness(material_thickness)
23
material.setConductivity(material_conductivity)
24
material.setDensity(material_density)
25
material.setSpecificHeat(material_specific_heat)
26
material.setThermalAbsorptance(material_thermal_absorptance)
27
material.setSolarAbsorptance(material_solar_absorptance)
28
29
30
material_list.append(material)
31
32
return material_list
33
34
# Definition to create a construction from a given set of material layers
35
'''
36
Description: This function creates a construction with a given set of material
layers. The function returns a construction , which can be used to create
a surface.
37
Input: model, name, materials layers
38
Output: construction
39
'''
40
def create_construction(model, name, materials):
41
construction = op.model.Construction(model)
42
construction.setName(name)
43
# Add material layers from materials list
44
for index, material in enumerate(materials):
45
construction.insertLayer(index, material)
46
47
return construction
48

# Definition of the function to add life cycle costs to the model
4
'''
5
Description: This function adds life cycle costs to the model. The function
returns a life cycle costs object , which can be used to add life cycle
costs to different components.
6
Inputs:
7
- model: op.model.Model
8
- construction_cost: float
9
- electricity_cost: float
10
- natural_gas_cost: float
11
Outputs:
12
- lifecycle_costs: op.model.LifeCycleCosts
13
'''
14
def add_lcc(model, construction_cost , electricity_cost , natural_gas_cost):
15
# Get the life cycle costs object
16
lifecycle_costs = model.getLifeCycleCosts()
17
18
# Set up the currency for the costs
19
currency = op.CurrencyType('DKK')
# Specify the currency as Danish Krone
20
lifecycle_costs.setCurrency(currency)
21
22
# Create life cycle cost objects for different components
23
construction_cost = 100000
# Example construction cost in DKK
24
construction_lcc = lifecycle_costs.addLifeCycleCost('Construction',
construction_cost)
25
26
# Set up energy cost resources and escalation rates
27
electricity_resource = model.getElectricityResource()
28
electricity_lcc = lifecycle_costs.addLifeCycleCost('Electricity', 0)
#
Set initial cost to 0
29
electricity_lcc.setCostUnits('kWh')
# Set the cost units to kilowatt -
hours
110
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:125==
30
electricity_lcc.setUsePriceEscalation('Electricity', electricity_resource)
31
32
natural_gas_resource = model.getNaturalGasResource()
33
natural_gas_lcc = lifecycle_costs.addLifeCycleCost('NaturalGas', 0)
# Set
initial cost to 0
34
natural_gas_lcc.setCostUnits('Therms')
# Set the cost units to therms
35
natural_gas_lcc.setUsePriceEscalation('NaturalGas', natural_gas_resource)
36
37
# Apply the life cycle costs to the model
38
lifecycle_costs.applyCosts()
39
return lifecycle_costs

# Set the occupancy schedule
8
'''
9
Description: This function creates a schedule for the occupancy of the
building. The schedule is based on the following assumptions:
10
- The building is occupied from 6 AM to 6 PM on weekdays
11
- The building is occupied from 0 AM to 12 AM on weekends
12
- The building is unoccupied at all other times
13
'''
14
def set_occupancy_schedule(model):
15
default_schedule_type_limits = op.model.ScheduleTypeLimits(model)
16
default_schedule_type_limits.setLowerLimitValue(0)
17
default_schedule_type_limits.setUpperLimitValue(1)
18
default_schedule_type_limits.setNumericType("Discrete")
19
default_schedule_type_limits.setUnitType("Dimensionless")
20
default_schedule_type_limits.setName("Occupancy Fraction Schedule Type
Limits")
21
22
occupancy_schedule = op.model.ScheduleRuleset(model)
23
occupancy_schedule.setName("Occupancy Fraction Schedule")
24
occupancy_schedule.setScheduleTypeLimits(default_schedule_type_limits)
25
26
# Create a rule for the default schedule (1 = occupied , 0 = unoccupied)
27
# 0 - 6 AM occupied
28
# 6 AM - 6 PM unoccupied
29
# 6 PM - 12 AM occupied
30
default_day = occupancy_schedule.defaultDaySchedule()
31
default_day.addValue(op.Time(0, 0, 0, 0), 0)
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
129
==PAGE:144==
32
default_day.addValue(op.Time(0, 6, 0, 0), 1)
33
default_day.addValue(op.Time(0, 18, 0, 0), 0)
34
default_day.addValue(op.Time(0, 24, 0, 0), 1)
35
36
# Create a rule for the weekend schedule (1 = occupied , 0 = unoccupied)
37
# occupied all day
38
weekend_rule = op.model.ScheduleRule(occupancy_schedule)
39
weekend_rule.setName("Weekend")
40
weekend_rule.setApplySaturday(True)
41
weekend_rule.setApplySunday(True)
42
43
weekend_day = weekend_rule.daySchedule()
44
weekend_day.addValue(op.Time(0, 0, 0, 0), 1)
45
weekend_day.addValue(op.Time(0, 24, 0, 0), 1)
46
47
return occupancy_schedule

# Definition to calculate U-value given a list of materials and thicknesses
277
'''
278
Description: This function calculates the U-value given a list of materials
and thicknesses
279
Inputs:
280
- materials: list of op.model.Material
281
- thicknesses: list of float
282
Outputs:
283
- U_value: float
284
'''
285
def calculate_U_value(materials , thicknesses):
286
# Calculate the U-value
287
U_value = 0
288
for material , thickness in zip(materials , thicknesses):
289
U_value += thickness / material.thermalConductivity()

U_value = 1 / U_value
291
return U_value

# Define a functions that calculates the apparent temperature
2
def apparent_temperature(T_a, e, ws=0.0):
3
return T_a + 0.33 * e - 0.7 * ws - 4.0
4
5
def absolute_humidity(T_a, rh):
6
return rh/100 * 6.105 * np.exp(17.27 * T_a / (237.7 + T_a))

def get_features_sets(selector):

occupancy_detailed', 'equipment', 'hvac', 'lighting', 'infiltration',

building_geometry', 'building_construction_simple', 'building_envelope
', 'occupancy_simple', 'equipment', 'hvac', 'day_period', '
history_temperature'], "Aggregated": ['weather', 'building_geometry',
'building_construction_simple', 'occupancy_simple', 'day_period', '
history_temperature'], "Highly -Aggregated": ['weather', 'type_house',
'day_period', 'history_temperature'], "all" : ['all']}

def get_data(scenario , features , target , scaler=False, periods=[]):

46
# Merge data and inputs given key and index

48
49
# Filter data on Datetime and keep year 2023
50

51

52
# Filter data on Datetime and keep the periods of interest stored in
period under the form of a list of two datetime objects [start, end]
53
if periods != []:
54
filtered_data = pd.DataFrame()
55
for period in periods: # Assuming periods is your list of periods
56
start_date = pd.to_datetime(period[0])
57
end_date = pd.to_datetime(period[1])
58

Datetime'] <= end_date)]

60

61
62

Mean Air Temperature'], rh=row['Indoor Air Relative Humidity'], v=0, q
=0), axis=1)

64

65

evening' if x >= 18 and x < 22 else 'night')))
67
68

< 40 else ( 1 if x >= 27 and x < 32 else 0))))
69
70
# Group values in data['Wind Direction'] into 8 categories N, NE, E, SE, S
, SW, W, NW
71
data['Wind Direction'] = data['Wind Direction'].apply(lambda x: 'N' if x
>= 337.5 or x < 22.5 else ('NE' if x >= 22.5 and x < 67.5 else ('E' if
x >= 67.5 and x < 112.5 else ('SE' if x >= 112.5 and x < 157.5 else (
'S' if x >= 157.5 and x < 202.5 else ('SW' if x >= 202.5 and x < 247.5
else ('W' if x >= 247.5 and x < 292.5 else 'NW')))))))
72
73
# Encode categorical features

le = LabelEncoder()
75
76

heat_stress_category', 'Wind Direction', 'construction_type', '
orientation', 'Wind Direction', 'ceiling_insulation', 'wall_U_value',
'roof_U_value', 'ground_U_value']
77
exception_features = ['heat_stress_category']
78
for feature in categorical_features:
79
if feature not in exception_features:
80
data[feature] = le.fit_transform(data[feature])

# save the encoder in a json file

encoder_path = '../results/models/' + scenario + '_' + feature +
148
Using AI to predict indoor temperature to improve indoor thermal quality
==PAGE:163==
'_encoder.json'

# Function to balance the data
100
'''
101
Description: This function returns the balanced data.
102
Inputs: X (DataFrame): DataFrame with the features
103
y (Series): Series with the target variable
104
Outputs: X (DataFrame): DataFrame with the features
105
y (Series): Series with the target variable
106
'''
107
def balance_data(X, y):
108
from sklearn.utils import resample
109
min_class_size = y.value_counts().min()
110
# Create balanced dataset
111
df_balanced = pd.concat([X, y], axis=1)
112
113
# Join X and y to create a balanced dataset
114
df = pd.concat([X, y], axis=1)
115
116
for class_ in y.unique():
117
class_samples = df[df['heat_stress_category'] == class_]
118
class_samples_downsampled = resample(class_samples , replace=False,
n_samples=min_class_size , random_state=42)
119
df_balanced = pd.concat([df_balanced , class_samples_downsampled])
120
121
X = df_balanced.drop('heat_stress_category', axis=1)
122
y = df_balanced['heat_stress_category']
123
124
return X, y

def apply_pca_timeseries(df, features , n_components=2, time_series_data=[],
pca_data=[]):

64

65
('standardize', StandardScaler()),

('pca', PCA(n_components=n_components)),
67
])

pca_data.append(transformed_data[:, 0])

time_series_data.append(transformed_data)
72
return time_series_data , pca_data

def import_saws_data(filename):

except:
36
pass

def calculate_U_value(list):
40
resistance = []
41
for i in list:
42
thickness = i[1]
43
conductivity = i[2]
44
